The Objection of Command Without Intention
Commentator refutes an objection about command without intention to obey.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the reality of Allah's speech against the objection that a command can be uttered without intending the commanded action.

Core Idea
A command is real even if the speaker does not intend the external action — the act is the utterance itself.

In this passage, an objection is raised: what if someone gives a command but explicitly states they do not want the action to be done? Does that still count as a real command? The commentator clarifies that the 'act' in command refers to the utterance of the imperative sentence, and its immediate effect is to cause belief, not necessarily to produce the external action. This defense reinforces the Ash'ari position that the speech of Allah is eternal and real, not dependent on external intentions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the full literal translation to understand the flow of the objection and answer.
Notice the hypothetical scenario introduced by the objector.
2
Identify the objection scenario
The objector presents a case: a speaker says to a third party that he is commanding someone but does not want the act done, then commands in that person's presence. Identify why this is seen as problematic.
The objector argues that if command can exist without volition, then the existence of a verbal command does not prove a psychological command.
3
Understand the commentator's answer
The commentator clarifies that the 'act' in the command form refers to the utterance of the imperative sentence itself, not the external action. Also, being a cause for belief means without considering external cues.
This shows that the command is still real even if the speaker does not intend the external act.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this defense protects the reality of Allah's command and speech. The objection does not invalidate the existence of psychological speech.
The commentator concludes with 'fa-ta'amal' indicating careful reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وليس يتجه عليه إِلخ) فإن قلت يرد عليه أنه وإن لم يتجه عليه ذلك لكن يتجه عليه أن الأمر قد يوجد في صورة لا يوجد فيها إرادة فعل يصير سبباً لاعتقاد المخاطب إرادة المتكلم لما أمر به كما إذا قال المتكلم لرجل : أنا أمرك عند فلان بالأمر الفلاني لكن لا أريد منك الإتيان به وإنما أمرك به لأجله، ثم يقول له بمحضره افعل كذا لا يقال ليس هناك حقيقة الأمر لأنا نقول : فكذلك في الصورة التي ذكرت فيما سبق فلا ترجيح لأحد القولين على الآخر قلت : المراد من الفعل في صورة الأمر الإتيان بالجملة الأمرية، وبكونه سبباً لما ذكر كونه سبباً مع قطع النظر عن القرائن الخارجية فتأمل.
His statement: 'And it does not apply to him etc.' Then you say: It is objected against him that even if that does not apply, yet it applies that the command may exist in a form in which there is no volition of an act that becomes a cause for the addressee's belief in the speaker's will for what is commanded, as when the speaker says to a man: 'I command you in the presence of so-and-so with such-and-such command, but I do not want you to perform it, I only command you for his sake,' then he says in his presence: 'Do such-and-such.' It cannot be said that there is no reality of command, for we say: likewise in the form mentioned previously, so there is no preference for one of the two statements over the other. I say: The intended meaning of 'act' in the form of command is the utterance of the imperative sentence, and that it is a cause as mentioned, being a cause without consideration of external cues, so ponder.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents an additional objection and its answer. The objection imagines a scenario where a speaker says to a third party: 'I command you in front of so-and-so with such-and-such, but I do not want you to do it; I only command for his sake.' Then in the presence of that person, he says 'Do such-and-such.' The objector argues that here there is no genuine command (no real will), so the existence of the verbal form alone does not prove a psychological command. The answer (by the commentator) clarifies that in the command form, the 'act' (الفعل) refers to the utterance of the imperative sentence itself, not the external action. Moreover, being a cause for belief (باعتقاد) means it serves as a cause when external cues are ignored. Therefore, even in this scenario, the imperative utterance is still a command in terms of its psychological reality, because it is uttered with the intention to cause belief in the listener (the third party). The commentator concludes that the objection does not invalidate the existence of psychological speech.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that command always requires a desire for the external action.
Correction: This commentary shows that the act in command is the utterance itself, and its effect can be to cause belief, not necessarily to bring about the action.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The command's reality lies in the utterance itself, not in the speaker's desire for the external action, thus preserving the reality of Allah's speech.

The Point of Dispute on Divine Speech
Al-Jurjani demarcates the exact point of dispute on divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise point of disagreement between Ash'aris and Mu'tazilites regarding divine speech, and why the concept of psychic speech is central to the debate.

Core Idea
The dispute is not about created speech but about an eternal psychic attribute.

Al-Jurjani begins by clearing common ground: both schools accept that the spoken Qur'an — the sounds and letters — is created. The real disagreement lies elsewhere. This passage pinpoints the exact node of contention, which is the Ash'ari doctrine of psychic speech (kalam nafsi). Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the broader theological debate on God's speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Al-Jurjani first states what both sides agree on before introducing the dispute.
Pay attention to the structure: agreement → disputed point.
2
Identify the agreed point
Highlight that both Ash'aris and Mu'tazilites affirm that the sounds and letters of the Qur'an are created (hadith) and subsist in something other than God. This is not in dispute.
3
Find the disputed point
The passage says the dispute is over the negation and affirmation of 'the psychic attribute' (al-ma'na al-nafsi). Locate this phrase and understand that it refers to an eternal inner speech distinct from knowledge and will.
4
Understand the implications
Reflect on why this matters: the Ash'aris affirm an eternal attribute of speech, while Mu'tazilites deny any such attribute beyond God's knowledge. This shapes their views on the Qur'an's eternity.
5
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you can identify the core issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا الذي قررناه لك فاعلم أن ما تقول المعتزلة في كلام الله تعالى وهو خلق الأصوات والحروف الدالة على المعاني المقصودة، وكونها حادثة قائمة بغير ذاته تعالى فنحن نقول به ولا نزاع بيننا وبينهم في ذلك كما مر آنفا وما نقوله نحن ونثبته من كلام النفس المغاير لسائر الصفات فهم ينكرون ثبوته ولو سلموه لم ينفوا قدمه الذي ندعيه في كلامه تعالى فصار محل النزاع بيننا وبينهم نفي المعنى النفسي وإثباته
When you have understood this which we have established for you, know that what the Mu'tazilites say about the speech of God Almighty — namely that it is the creation of sounds and letters indicating intended meanings, and that it is originated (hadith) and subsisting in something other than His essence — we affirm it, and there is no dispute between us and them in that, as has just passed. And what we say and affirm — namely the psychic speech (kalam nafsi) distinct from the other attributes — they deny its existence, and even if they granted it, they would not deny its eternity (qidam) which we claim for His speech. So the point of dispute between us and them became the negation and affirmation of the psychic attribute (al-ma'na al-nafsi).
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage defines the exact point of disagreement between the Ash'ari school and the Mu'tazilites regarding divine speech. Al-Jurjani first clarifies a point of agreement: both schools accept that the spoken Qur'an — the sounds and letters that convey meanings — is created (makhluq) and originated in time. The Ash'aris do not deny this. The real controversy concerns what the Ash'aris call 'psychic speech' (kalam nafsi), an eternal attribute of God that is the inner meaning expressed by the sounds and letters. The Mu'tazilites deny the existence of any such attribute beyond God's knowledge and will. Al-Jurjani argues that even if the Mu'tazilites admitted the existence of psychic speech, they would still reject its eternity — but the main point is that the dispute is specifically about whether there is a psychic attribute distinct from other attributes.

معنى نفسي
خلق الأصوات والحروف

الحادث

Correction: This passage clarifies that Ash'aris agree with Mu'tazilites that the spoken words (sounds and letters) are created; the dispute is about a distinct eternal attribute of psychic speech.

C) Whether there exists a psychic attribute of speech distinct from knowledge and will
D) Whether divine speech is originated

Key Takeaway
The dispute between Ash'aris and Mu'tazilites on divine speech is specifically about the existence of an eternal psychic speech attribute, not about the createdness of the spoken Qur'an.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage shows that the Ash'ari position includes affirming a psychic attribute that is distinct from God's knowledge and will. Does your heart truly distinguish between God's speech as an attribute and His other attributes, or do you unknowingly reduce His speech to just another form of knowledge? Take a moment to ask: do I really experience the Qur'an as a direct address from God, or merely as information?
?
Al-Jurjani says the dispute is 'just' about affirming or negating this psychic attribute. But if you deny it, you reduce God's speech to created phenomena. Examine your own soul: do you sometimes treat the Qur'an as a mere human creation in terms of how you prioritize listening to it, memorizing it, or acting on it? What is one concrete change you can make this week to honor the reality of God's eternal speech?

The Two Meanings of 'Meaning' and the Role of Verbal Speech
Author distinguishes two senses of 'meaning' and discusses sign-relationship between verbal and psychological speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between two meanings of 'meaning' (المعنى) and why the truthfulness of verbal speech is necessary for it to be a sign of psychological speech.

Core Idea
Verbal speech must be truthful to serve as a sign of psychological speech.

This lesson unpacks the author's analysis of the term 'meaning' (المعنى). He shows that it is used in two senses: as the import of a word and as a quality subsisting in something. This distinction is crucial for understanding the Ash'ari doctrine of God's speech, where psychological speech is the eternal divine attribute, and expressions are merely human signs. We will see a circularity argument about the relationship between verbal and psychological speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the technical terms.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'meaning'
Find where the author distinguishes the two uses of 'meaning' and understand each.
The first is the import of the word; the second is a quality in another.
3
Trace the circularity argument
Follow the objection that building the truth of verbal speech on its being a sign of psychological speech is circular, and see how the companions resolve it.
The companions first establish the truth of verbal speech independently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن للمصنف مقالة مفردة في تحقيق كلام الله تعالى على وفق ما أشار إليه في خطبة الكتاب ومحصولها أن لفظ المعنى يطلق تارة على مدلول اللفظ وأخرى على الأمر القائم بالغير، فالشيخ الأشعري لما قال الكلام هو المعنى النفسي فهم الأصحاب منه أن مراده مدلول اللفظ وحده وهو القديم عنده، وأما العبارات كما مر . قوله : (مع أن الأهم عندنا هو بيان صدقها لأنها التي تنتظم بها المصالح الدينية والدنيوية ولا سبيل إلى معرفة الكلام النفسي جعل هذه الألفاظ والعبارات دوال بالنسبة إليه، ومن لوازم كونها دوال عليه وأنه لا كذب فيه أن لا يكون فيها أيضاً كذب إذ وقوع الكذب فيها دون النفسي يمنع كونها دوال عليه وفيه نظر لأن كون الألفاظ والعبارات دوال بالنسبة إلى الكلام النفسي الثابت صدقه بالدليل المذكور فرع امتناع الكذب في الكلام اللفظي، كما لا يخفى فبناء امتناع الكذب فيه على كونه دوال عليه دور والأصحاب إنما قالوا بكون الكلام اللفظي دليلا على المعنى النفسي القائم بذاته تعالى في نفس الأمر بعد ما تقرر عندهم من امتناع الكذب فيه، فليتأمل . قوله : ( فهو تصديق فعلي إلخ ) اعترض عليه بأن التصديق الفعلي إنما ينتهض حجة إذا لم يجوز النقص في فعله تعالى ولا فرق بينه وبين القبح العقلي كما مر آنفا، فلا ذلك الاستدلال أيضاً إلا مع القول بثبوت القبح العقلي وحينئذ فليتمسك ابتداء بما قال المعتزلة إسقاطاً لكثرة المؤونات وأخذ بالأسهل وجوابه أن حجية التصديق الفعلي معلومة بحسب العادة سواء جوز النقص في الفعل أم لا، ألا ترى أن قوماً إذا اجتمعوا عند ملك عظيم من ملوك الدنيا ثم قام واحد وقال : أيها الناس إني رسول هذا الملك إليكم ثم قال : أيها الملك إن كنت صادقا فيما قلته منهم فخالف عادتك في القيام والقعود فإذا فعل الملك ذلك اضطر الحاضرون إلى العلم بكونه صادقاً ولا يخطر ببالهم عدم جواز النقص في فعله قطعاً كيف ولا شك في الجواز في هذه الصورة أصلاً .
Know that the author has a separate treatise on the verification of God's speech, according to what he indicated in the preface of the book. Its summary is that the term 'meaning' is used sometimes for the import of the word and sometimes for the thing subsisting in something else. When al-Ash'ari said 'speech is psychological meaning,' the companions understood that he intended the import of the word alone, which is eternal according to him. As for the expressions, as has passed. His statement: 'Although the most important for us is to show its truth, because it is by it that religious and worldly interests are organized, and there is no way to know psychological speech except by making these words and expressions signs with respect to it. And from the necessities of their being signs for it and that there is no lie in it is that there should also be no lie in them, because the occurrence of a lie in them without the psychological would prevent them from being signs of it. But there is a problem: the fact that words and expressions are signs with respect to psychological speech whose truth is established by the mentioned proof is a branch of the impossibility of lying in verbal speech, as is not hidden. So building the impossibility of lying in it on its being a sign of it is circular. The companions only said that verbal speech is a proof of the psychological meaning subsisting in God's essence in reality after it was established for them the impossibility of lying in it. So ponder.' His statement: 'So it is a practical confirmation, etc.' An objection was raised against it: practical confirmation is only valid as a proof if deficiency in His action is not permissible, and there is no difference between it and rational ugliness as passed. Thus, that argument is also only valid with affirming rational ugliness, and then one should initially take what the Mu'tazila said, dropping many difficulties and taking the easier. The answer is that the validity of practical confirmation is known by custom, whether deficiency in action is permissible or not. Do you not see that if people gather before a great king of the kings of the world, then one stands and says, 'O people, I am the messenger of this king to you,' then says, 'O king, if I am truthful in what I said about you, then break your custom in standing and sitting.' When the king does that, the attendees are compelled to know that he is truthful, and it does not occur to their minds the impermissibility of deficiency in his action at all. How could there be doubt about its permissibility in this case originally?
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage contains two main parts. First, it explains the author's (al-Iji's) own view on the nature of God's speech. The author distinguishes two meanings of the word 'meaning' (ma'na): (1) the import of the word, and (2) the thing subsisting in another. According to al-Ash'ari, speech is psychological meaning, and the Ash'ari companions understood this as the import of the word alone, which is eternal. Then it discusses the relationship between verbal expressions and psychological meaning. Some argue that for verbal expressions to be signs of the psychological meaning, they must themselves be truthful; otherwise, the sign would be corrupted. But this reasoning is circular because it assumes what is to be proved (the truthfulness of verbal speech). The companions instead hold that verbal speech is a proof of the psychological meaning after it is established that there is no lie in verbal speech. The second part responds to an objection about practical confirmation (miracle) as evidence for the prophet's truthfulness. The objection says that practical confirmation assumes the impermissibility of deficiency in God's action, which is the same as rational ugliness—a concept the Ash'aris reject. The answer is that the validity of practical confirmation is based on custom (not rational necessity). The example of the king shows that people naturally infer truthfulness from an extraordinary act without considering whether deficiency is permissible. Thus, the argument stands regardless of the denial of rational ugliness.

Key Terms
المعنى
مدلول اللفظ
المعنى النفسي
دوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What did the companions understand al-Ash'ari's 'psychological speech' to be?
A) The verbal expression itself
B) The import of the word (مدلول اللفظ)
C) A contingent attribute
D) The action of speaking

Key Takeaway
The truthfulness of verbal speech is a prerequisite for it to be a sign of psychological speech, and not derived from it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat God's speech in revelation (the Qur'an and authentic Sunnah) as a truthful sign of His will, or do you allow your own desires to distort its meaning, effectively making it a 'lie' in your heart? How will you actively guard against this?
?
When you encounter a difficult theological concept like the circularity of signs, do you dismiss it as irrelevant to your faith, or do you recognize that understanding such intricacies protects you from speaking falsely about Allah? What step can you take today to deepen your understanding of what Allah's speech truly requires of you?
?
If the verbal speech of the Qur'an is a sign of God's psychological speech, and you know that sign must be truthful, what does that demand from you when you recite or interpret the Qur'an? Does your life bear witness to its truth, or are you inadvertently misrepresenting Him by your actions?

Practical Confirmation as Customary Evidence for Prophethood
Author refutes the claim that practical confirmation requires rational ugliness by showing its basis in custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how practical confirmation (المعجزة) serves as evidence for a prophet's truthfulness based on custom, not rational ugliness.

Core Idea
Miracles prove prophethood through customary inference, not rational necessity.

This lesson addresses an objection against using miracles (practical confirmation) as proof of prophethood. The objection claims that miracles only work if one accepts rational ugliness (that God cannot do a deceptive act). The author replies that miracles are validated by custom, not rational ugliness. He gives an example of a king breaking his custom to confirm a messenger, showing that people intuitively accept such signs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the objection and the answer.
2
Understand the objection against practical confirmation
Identify why the objector thinks practical confirmation requires rational ugliness.
The objector says it's only valid if deficiency in God's action is not permissible.
3
Analyze the author's answer
See how the author rejects the need for rational ugliness by grounding the proof in custom.
The answer states that practical confirmation's validity is known by custom, regardless of rational ugliness.
4
Reflect on the king example
Understand how the example illustrates customary inference without considering deficiency.
The people are compelled to know the messenger is truthful by the king's action, without thinking about deficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن للمصنف مقالة مفردة في تحقيق كلام الله تعالى على وفق ما أشار إليه في خطبة الكتاب ومحصولها أن لفظ المعنى يطلق تارة على مدلول اللفظ وأخرى على الأمر القائم بالغير، فالشيخ الأشعري لما قال الكلام هو المعنى النفسي فهم الأصحاب منه أن مراده مدلول اللفظ وحده وهو القديم عنده، وأما العبارات كما مر . قوله : (مع أن الأهم عندنا هو بيان صدقها لأنها التي تنتظم بها المصالح الدينية والدنيوية ولا سبيل إلى معرفة الكلام النفسي جعل هذه الألفاظ والعبارات دوال بالنسبة إليه، ومن لوازم كونها دوال عليه وأنه لا كذب فيه أن لا يكون فيها أيضاً كذب إذ وقوع الكذب فيها دون النفسي يمنع كونها دوال عليه وفيه نظر لأن كون الألفاظ والعبارات دوال بالنسبة إلى الكلام النفسي الثابت صدقه بالدليل المذكور فرع امتناع الكذب في الكلام اللفظي، كما لا يخفى فبناء امتناع الكذب فيه على كونه دوال عليه دور والأصحاب إنما قالوا بكون الكلام اللفظي دليلا على المعنى النفسي القائم بذاته تعالى في نفس الأمر بعد ما تقرر عندهم من امتناع الكذب فيه، فليتأمل . قوله : ( فهو تصديق فعلي إلخ ) اعترض عليه بأن التصديق الفعلي إنما ينتهض حجة إذا لم يجوز النقص في فعله تعالى ولا فرق بينه وبين القبح العقلي كما مر آنفا، فلا ذلك الاستدلال أيضاً إلا مع القول بثبوت القبح العقلي وحينئذ فليتمسك ابتداء بما قال المعتزلة إسقاطاً لكثرة المؤونات وأخذ بالأسهل وجوابه أن حجية التصديق الفعلي معلومة بحسب العادة سواء جوز النقص في الفعل أم لا، ألا ترى أن قوماً إذا اجتمعوا عند ملك عظيم من ملوك الدنيا ثم قام واحد وقال : أيها الناس إني رسول هذا الملك إليكم ثم قال : أيها الملك إن كنت صادقا فيما قلته منهم فخالف عادتك في القيام والقعود فإذا فعل الملك ذلك اضطر الحاضرون إلى العلم بكونه صادقاً ولا يخطر ببالهم عدم جواز النقص في فعله قطعاً كيف ولا شك في الجواز في هذه الصورة أصلاً .
Know that the author has a separate treatise on the verification of God's speech, according to what he indicated in the preface of the book. Its summary is that the term 'meaning' is used sometimes for the import of the word and sometimes for the thing subsisting in something else. When al-Ash'ari said 'speech is psychological meaning,' the companions understood that he intended the import of the word alone, which is eternal according to him. As for the expressions, as has passed. His statement: 'Although the most important for us is to show its truth, because it is by it that religious and worldly interests are organized, and there is no way to know psychological speech except by making these words and expressions signs with respect to it. And from the necessities of their being signs for it and that there is no lie in it is that there should also be no lie in them, because the occurrence of a lie in them without the psychological would prevent them from being signs of it. But there is a problem: the fact that words and expressions are signs with respect to psychological speech whose truth is established by the mentioned proof is a branch of the impossibility of lying in verbal speech, as is not hidden. So building the impossibility of lying in it on its being a sign of it is circular. The companions only said that verbal speech is a proof of the psychological meaning subsisting in God's essence in reality after it was established for them the impossibility of lying in it. So ponder.' His statement: 'So it is a practical confirmation, etc.' An objection was raised against it: practical confirmation is only valid as a proof if deficiency in His action is not permissible, and there is no difference between it and rational ugliness as passed. Thus, that argument is also only valid with affirming rational ugliness, and then one should initially take what the Mu'tazila said, dropping many difficulties and taking the easier. The answer is that the validity of practical confirmation is known by custom, whether deficiency in action is permissible or not. Do you not see that if people gather before a great king of the kings of the world, then one stands and says, 'O people, I am the messenger of this king to you,' then says, 'O king, if I am truthful in what I said about you, then break your custom in standing and sitting.' When the king does that, the attendees are compelled to know that he is truthful, and it does not occur to their minds the impermissibility of deficiency in his action at all. How could there be doubt about its permissibility in this case originally?
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage contains two main parts. First, it explains the author's (al-Iji's) own view on the nature of God's speech. The author distinguishes two meanings of the word 'meaning' (ma'na): (1) the import of the word, and (2) the thing subsisting in another. According to al-Ash'ari, speech is psychological meaning, and the Ash'ari companions understood this as the import of the word alone, which is eternal. Then it discusses the relationship between verbal expressions and psychological meaning. Some argue that for verbal expressions to be signs of the psychological meaning, they must themselves be truthful; otherwise, the sign would be corrupted. But this reasoning is circular because it assumes what is to be proved (the truthfulness of verbal speech). The companions instead hold that verbal speech is a proof of the psychological meaning after it is established that there is no lie in verbal speech. The second part responds to an objection about practical confirmation (miracle) as evidence for the prophet's truthfulness. The objection says that practical confirmation assumes the impermissibility of deficiency in God's action, which is the same as rational ugliness—a concept the Ash'aris reject. The answer is that the validity of practical confirmation is based on custom (not rational necessity). The example of the king shows that people naturally infer truthfulness from an extraordinary act without considering whether deficiency is permissible. Thus, the argument stands regardless of the denial of rational ugliness.

تصديق فعلي
دلالة عادية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles require rational ugliness to function as proof.
Correction: The author shows miracles are based on custom; people infer truthfulness from extraordinary acts without needing rational ugliness.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the king example show practical confirmation works?
A) Because the king's action is rationally necessary
B) Because custom compels recognition of truth
C) Because deficiency is impossible
D) Because the messenger is known to be honest

Key Takeaway
Miracles serve as evidence for prophethood based on customary human reasoning, not on the assumption of rational ugliness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a clear sign from God—like the preservation of the Qur'an or the beauty of creation—does your heart feel compelled to believe, or do you find excuses? What prevents you from having the certainty of the king's audience?
?
The objector thought the Mu'tazilite position was easier. Are there areas in your faith where you accept a simpler but flawed explanation because it feels more comfortable, rather than the deeper truth that requires humility? What is one such area?
?
The king's custom-breaking made his messenger's truth undeniable. Has there been a moment in your life when God 'broke His custom' for you—an answered prayer or a situation only He could orchestrate—yet you still doubted? How will you respond to that memory today?

The Fallacy of Denying Attributes from Lack of Evidence
Refutation of the argument from silence regarding additional divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the absence of evidence for an attribute does not prove its non-existence, and the logical principle that the non-existence of a necessary condition does not entail the non-existence of its necessary consequence.

Core Idea
Absence of evidence is not evidence of absence in theology.

This lesson examines a key debate about whether Allah has attributes beyond the seven well-known ones. Some argued that without evidence, we should deny such attributes. But this passage shows that logic itself rejects that move. It introduces a crucial principle: the non-existence of a necessary condition does not prove the non-existence of its necessary consequence. Understanding this strengthens theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the overall argument. Let the author's flow come through before you analyze.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two sides
The passage presents a denial based on lack of evidence and a refutation using logical principles. Mark where each side appears.
The denial is from 'some of our companions'; the refutation begins with 'And its weakness is not hidden...'
3
Focus on the key logical principle
Understand the statement: 'the non-existence of the necessary condition does not necessitate the non-existence of its necessary consequence'. Ask yourself what this means in context.
A necessary condition is something that must be present for the consequence to occur, but its absence doesn't automatically remove the consequence.
4
Consider the two interpretations
The commentary offers two ways to understand the principle. Reflect on how both lead to the same conclusion: absence of evidence is not sufficient for denial unless the lack is exhaustive.
This connects to the broader theme of theological methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثامن : في أنه تعالى صفة ( في صفات اختلف فيها وفيه مقدمة ومسائل) إحدى عشرة ( فالمقدمة : ) هي أنه ( هل لله تعالى صفة ) وجودية زائدة على ذاته ( غير ما ذكرناه ) من الصفات السبع الحياة والعلم والقدرة والإرادة والسمع والبصر والكلام، فمنعه بعض أصحابنا مقتصراً) في نفيها ( على أنه لا دليل عليه ) أي على ثبوت صفة أخرى ( فيجب نفيه ولا يخفى ضعفه لما مر من أن عدم الدليل عندك لا يفيد عدمه في ) نفس الأمر ممنوع وإن سلم لم يفد أيضا لأن انتفاء الملزوم لا يستلزم انتفاء لازمه، وهذا الذي ذكرناه وإن كان مخالفا لما عليه متأخرو أصحابنا لا أنه بعد التأمل تعرف حقيقته ثم كلامه وهذا المحمل لكلام الشيخ مما اختاره الشيخ محمد الشهرستاني في كتابه المسمى بنهاية الإقدام ولا شبهة في أنه أقرب إلى الأحكام الظاهرية المنسوبة إلى قواعد الملة . قوله : ( لأن انتفاء الملزوم لا يستلزم انتفاء (لازمه) يحتمل أن يحمل على حذف المضاف أي لا يستلزم انتفاء متعلق لازمه وذلك لأن المراد هاهنا نفي لزوم انتفاء ثبوت الصفة، ولا شك أن الدليل على تقدير وجوده يستلزم التصديق بذلك الثبوت ويحتمل أن يبقى على ظاهره لأن الدليل كالعالم قد يستلزم نفس المدلول كوجود الصانع وإن لم يكن محصلا له في الواقع، وعلى كل تقدير لا أن انتفاء ملزوم خاص وإن لم يستلزم انتفاء اللازم مطلقاً إلا أن انتفاء الملزوم يرد بالكلية يستلزم انتفاء اللازم فيلزم انتفاء صفة لم يقم عليها دليل أما على التقدير الأول فظاهر وأما على التقدير الثاني فلأنه لا يلزم من نفي الملزوم الذي لا مدخل له في حصول لازمة نفي اللازم كما مر في جواب تاسع شبه السمنية على فائدة النظر العلم .
The eighth objective: concerning that He, exalted, has an attribute. (Concerning attributes about which there is disagreement, and it includes a preface and eleven issues) (The preface:) it is whether Allah, exalted, has a positive attribute additional to His essence other than what we have mentioned of the seven attributes: life, knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, and speech. Some of our companions denied it, limiting themselves in its negation to the statement that there is no evidence for it, i.e., for the existence of another attribute, so its negation is necessary. And its weakness is not hidden due to what has passed: that the lack of evidence in your view does not necessitate its non-existence in reality, which is denied; and even if granted, it would not be beneficial either because the non-existence of the necessary condition does not necessitate the non-existence of its necessary consequence. And this which we have mentioned, although it opposes what the later scholars among our companions hold, yet upon reflection its reality becomes known, and this interpretation of the Shaykh's words is what Shaykh Muhammad al-Shahrastani chose in his book called Nihayat al-Iqdam, and there is no doubt that it is closer to the apparent rulings attributed to the foundations of the religion. His saying: 'Because the non-existence of the necessary condition does not necessitate the non-existence of its necessary consequence' – it is possible that it is interpreted as an ellipsis of the genitive complement, meaning it does not necessitate the non-existence of the object of the necessary consequence. And that is because the intent here is the negation of the necessity of the non-existence of the affirmation of the attribute, and there is no doubt that the evidence, if it exists, necessitates belief in that affirmation. And it is possible that it remains on its apparent meaning because evidence, like a cause, may necessitate the thing itself, like the existence of the Maker even if it does not produce it in reality. And in any case, it is not that the non-existence of a particular necessary condition does not necessitate the non-existence of the necessary consequence absolutely; rather, the non-existence of the necessary condition that is essential necessitates the non-existence of the necessary consequence, so it necessitates the non-existence of an attribute for which no evidence has been established. As for the first interpretation, it is apparent; as for the second interpretation, it is because it does not follow from the negation of the necessary condition that has no role in the occurrence of its necessary consequence that the necessary consequence is negated, as passed in the response to the ninth doubt of the Samaniyya regarding the benefit of theoretical reflection.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage defines the first question of the eighth objective: whether Allah has additional attributes beyond the seven. It presents the position of some scholars who deny additional attributes solely on the grounds that there is no evidence for them. The commentator immediately refutes this, noting that the absence of evidence in one's own knowledge does not prove non-existence in reality. Even if it were granted, the logical point is made: the non-existence of a necessary condition (muqlizum) does not entail the non-existence of its necessary consequence (lazim). The commentator then explains this statement in detail. He considers two interpretations: (1) that the statement is elliptical, meaning the non-existence of the condition does not entail the non-existence of the object of the consequence; or (2) that it is literal, meaning evidence, like a cause, may entail the thing itself but does not produce it in reality. He then clarifies that the non-existence of a condition that is essential for the consequence does entail the non-existence of the consequence, so if no evidence exists for an attribute, it would be non-existent. However, the argument only works if the lack of evidence is total, not just personal. The commentator then relates this to a response against the Samaniyya skeptics.

صفة زائدة
الملزوم
اللازم
عدم الدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If there is no evidence for something, it must not exist.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that lack of evidence in one's own knowledge does not prove non-existence in reality, and that the non-existence of a necessary condition does not entail the non-existence of its necessary consequence.

Checkpoint
What is the logical flaw in denying an attribute solely due to lack of evidence, according to the passage?
A) The attribute might be known through other means.
B) The lack of evidence in one's own knowledge does not prove its non-existence in reality.
C) The attribute is necessarily existent because it is mentioned in revelation.
D) The negation is a personal preference, not a proof.

Key Takeaway
The absence of evidence for a divine attribute does not justify its denial; only a complete lack of evidence for existence can do so.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
What if you are wrong about an attribute of Allah you denied based on your own limited knowledge? Are you willing to remain open to truths beyond your current understanding?
?
In your daily life, do you apply the same logical fallacy—assuming something is false because you see no proof? How can you train yourself to withhold judgment until evidence is truly exhaustive?

The Rational Proof that God Does Not Will the Impossible
The author proves God does not will impossible things using three rational arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot will what He knows to be impossible, based on the impossibility of change in divine knowledge and the concept of preference-giving attribute.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of impossibility makes willing that thing impossible.

The author begins by grounding the discussion of divine will in God's prior knowledge. He argues that since God knows that a disbeliever will never believe, that belief becomes impossible—for if it were to occur, God's knowledge would have to change into ignorance, which is inconceivable. He then presents two further arguments: one from the consequences of willing the impossible, and another from the nature of the one who knows impossibility. This short but dense passage establishes a central axiom of Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage uses very concise argumentation; each phrase packs a logical step.
2
Identify the three arguments
The passage contains three interconnected proofs. Find each one: (1) the impossibility of knowledge turning into ignorance, (2) the dilemma if God were to will the impossible, and (3) the absence of a preference-giving attribute for the one who knows impossibility.
3
Understand the key terms
Focus on 'inqilāb al-ʿilm' (transformation of knowledge), 'muḥāl' (impossible), and 'ṣifat tarjīḥ' (preference-giving attribute). These are central to the proof.
4
Reflect on the discussion (fīhi baḥth)
The author notes an objection: the non-faith of the disbeliever is willed by God even though it is necessary. Then he gives a counterexample from the believer's faith. Ask yourself: does this weaken or strengthen the proof?
The discussion is dense; it helps to see it as a clarification of the scope of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الرابع : أنه تعالى مريد لجميع الكائنات غير مريد لما لا يكون فلأنه تعالى علم من الكافر مثلاً أنه لا يؤمن فكان الإيمان محالاً لامتناع أن ينقلب العلم جهلاً، والله تعالى عالم باستحالته والعالم باستحالة الشيء لا يريده بالضرورة، وأيضاً لو أراده فإما أن يقع فيلزم الانقلاب أولا فيلزم عجزه وقصوره عن تحقيق مراده ولأنه لا يتصور منه أي من العالم باستحالة الشيء صفة مرجحة لأحد طرفيه لأن أحدهما مستحيل والآخر واجب فلا معنى لترجيح الصفة، وفيه بحث لأن عدم إيمان الكافر مراد الله مع كونه واجباً وأيضاً هو منقوض بما علم الله وجوده كإيمان المؤمن فإن أحد طرفيه واجب والآخر ممتنع فلا لترجيح الصفة، ويعضد هذا الذي هو مذهبنا إجماع السلف والخلف في الأعصار والأمصار على إطلاق قولهم ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن فإن هذا مروي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وقد تلقته الأمة بالقبول فيصح أن يكون مؤيداً، بل ربما يحتج به أيضاً، وإنما صرح بالإطلاق دفعاً لتوهم التقييد بأفعاله تعالى أو بما ليس من أفعال العباد الاختيارية، كما تأوله به المعتزلة ويدفع هذا التوهم أنهم كانوا يوردون كلامهم في معرض تعظيم الله وإعلاء شأنه والأول : وهو ما شاء الله كان دليل الثاني وهو أنه تعالى غير مريد لما لا يكون، وذلك لأنه ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا : كل ما لم يكن لم يشأ الله . والثاني : أعني ما يشأ لم يكن دليل الأول لانعكاسه بذلك الطريق إلى قولنا كل ما كان فقد شاء الله
The sixth observation - the fourth objective: That He, exalted, is willing (murīd) of all beings, not willing of what is not. Because He, exalted, knew of the disbeliever, for example, that he will not believe, so faith became impossible (muḥāl) due to the impossibility of knowledge turning into ignorance. And God, exalted, is knowing of its impossibility, and the one who knows the impossibility of a thing does not will it necessarily. Also, if He willed it, then either it would occur, necessitating change (inqilāb), or it would not occur, necessitating His inability and deficiency in achieving His intention. And because from Him—i.e., from one who knows the impossibility of a thing—a preference-giving attribute (ṣifat tarjīḥ) for one of its two sides cannot be conceived, because one of them is impossible and the other is necessary, so there is no meaning to preferring by an attribute. And there is discussion in this because the non-faith of the disbeliever is willed by God despite it being necessary, and also it is refuted by what God knows to exist, like the faith of the believer, for one of its sides is necessary and the other is impossible, so there is no meaning to preferring by an attribute. And this, which is our doctrine, is supported by the consensus (ijmāʿ) of the predecessors and successors in ages and regions on their unrestricted statement: 'What God wills is, and what He does not will is not.' For this is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him), and the community received it with acceptance, so it is valid as a support. Rather, it may even be used as proof. And he explicitly stated the unrestrictedness to repel the misconception of restriction to His acts or to what is not from the voluntary acts of servants, as the Muʿtazila interpreted it. And this misconception is repelled by the fact that they used to utter their words in the context of glorifying God and exalting His status. And the first—i.e., 'What God wills is'—is evidence for the second—that He, exalted, is not willing of what is not—because it converts by contrapositive (ʿaks al-naqīḍ) to our saying: 'Everything that is not, God did not will.' And the second—I mean 'What He does not will is not'—is evidence for the first, by its conversion by that method to our saying: 'Everything that is, God has willed it.'
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God wills all things that exist and does not will what does not exist. It begins with a definition: God knows that the disbeliever will not believe, so faith is impossible because it would require God's knowledge to change into ignorance. Since God knows the impossibility of faith, He cannot will it—this is presented as necessary. A second argument: if God had willed faith for the disbeliever, it either would occur (necessitating a change in knowledge) or would not occur (implying God's inability). Third: from one who knows something is impossible, a preference-giving attribute cannot be conceived for one of its two sides, because one is impossible and the other necessary. The author then notes a discussion (فيه بحث): the non-faith of the disbeliever is willed by God despite being necessary, and this is refuted by God's knowledge of the believer's faith, which has one side necessary and the other impossible. After this, he supports the doctrine with consensus: the community's phrase 'What God wills is, what He does not will is not' is a decisive proof. He explains that this phrase is unrestricted to avoid the Muʿtazilite restriction to God's own acts or to non-voluntary acts. Finally, he shows the logical contrapositive: 'What God wills is' implies 'What is not, God did not will,' and 'What God does not will is not' implies 'What is, God has willed it.' This completes the proof.

Key Terms
إرادة
عالم بالاستحالة
محال
انقلاب العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can will impossible things if He chooses; His power is absolute.
Correction: The passage shows that willing the impossible leads to a contradiction: either divine knowledge changes or God is unable, both impossible for God. Therefore, God's will only pertains to what is possible.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what would happen if God were to will the impossible faith for the disbeliever?
A) It would definitely occur, proving God's power.
B) Either it would occur (requiring change in knowledge) or not occur (implying incapacity).
C) It would not occur, but God's will would remain intact.
D) The disbeliever would freely choose faith.

Key Takeaway
God's will is intrinsically tied to His knowledge: He cannot will what He knows to be impossible, as it would entail a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that God cannot will what He knows impossible. Reflect on a situation in your life where you desperately wanted something you knew, deep down, was not meant for you. How does knowing that God's will is guided by perfect knowledge—not arbitrary desire—change your trust in His plan for you? Will you today surrender a hidden desire that contradicts what you know God has decreed?
?
God knows that some people will never believe, yet He still wills their existence and their disbelief. How does this truth confront your heart when you think about people you have given up on—or people you resent for their choices? Can you accept that God's will is just and wise even when it allows others to persist in disbelief? What would it cost you to truly submit to that reality?
?
If God cannot will the impossible because it would imply deficiency, then what does your own insistence on impossible outcomes—whether in relationships, ambitions, or fears—say about your reliance on God? Are you willing to stop asking God for what He has already shown to be impossible and instead ask for the strength to accept His decree?

Consensus and Logical Structure of Divine Will
The author supports the doctrine with consensus and logical contrapositive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus of the community and the logical operation of contrapositive support the doctrine that God wills all that exists and does not will what does not exist.

Core Idea
The prophetic saying 'What God wills is' is a logical pivot for the entire doctrine.

After presenting rational arguments, the author now turns to transmitted evidence: the consensus of the Muslim community on the statement 'What God wills is, and what He does not will is not.' He explains that this statement, reported from the Prophet, has been universally accepted and can serve as a proof. Importantly, he clarifies that the statement is unrestricted, countering the Mu'tazilite attempt to limit it. He then demonstrates the logical power of this statement by showing how each half implies the other through contrapositive, thus unifying the entire doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay special attention to the logical terms 'عكس النقيض' (contrapositive).
2
Understand the consensus argument
The author cites the ijma' of the community on the saying 'ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن'. He notes it is narrated from the Prophet and accepted by all. Ask: why does he mention this after the rational proof?
Consensus provides additional authority and addresses potential objections.
3
Examine the refutation of Mu'tazilite restriction
The author explicitly states that the saying is unrestricted, rejecting the Mu'tazilite interpretation that limits it to God's own acts. He says this is to repel the misconception.
4
Follow the contrapositive reasoning
The author shows that 'What God wills is' converts to 'What is not, God did not will' and 'What He does not will is not' converts to 'What is, God has willed'. This is a logical equivalence.
This demonstrates the coherence of the doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الرابع : أنه تعالى مريد لجميع الكائنات غير مريد لما لا يكون فلأنه تعالى علم من الكافر مثلاً أنه لا يؤمن فكان الإيمان محالاً لامتناع أن ينقلب العلم جهلاً، والله تعالى عالم باستحالته والعالم باستحالة الشيء لا يريده بالضرورة، وأيضاً لو أراده فإما أن يقع فيلزم الانقلاب أولا فيلزم عجزه وقصوره عن تحقيق مراده ولأنه لا يتصور منه أي من العالم باستحالة الشيء صفة مرجحة لأحد طرفيه لأن أحدهما مستحيل والآخر واجب فلا معنى لترجيح الصفة، وفيه بحث لأن عدم إيمان الكافر مراد الله مع كونه واجباً وأيضاً هو منقوض بما علم الله وجوده كإيمان المؤمن فإن أحد طرفيه واجب والآخر ممتنع فلا لترجيح الصفة، ويعضد هذا الذي هو مذهبنا إجماع السلف والخلف في الأعصار والأمصار على إطلاق قولهم ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن فإن هذا مروي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وقد تلقته الأمة بالقبول فيصح أن يكون مؤيداً، بل ربما يحتج به أيضاً، وإنما صرح بالإطلاق دفعاً لتوهم التقييد بأفعاله تعالى أو بما ليس من أفعال العباد الاختيارية، كما تأوله به المعتزلة ويدفع هذا التوهم أنهم كانوا يوردون كلامهم في معرض تعظيم الله وإعلاء شأنه والأول : وهو ما شاء الله كان دليل الثاني وهو أنه تعالى غير مريد لما لا يكون، وذلك لأنه ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا : كل ما لم يكن لم يشأ الله . والثاني : أعني ما يشأ لم يكن دليل الأول لانعكاسه بذلك الطريق إلى قولنا كل ما كان فقد شاء الله
The sixth observation - the fourth objective: That He, exalted, is willing (murīd) of all beings, not willing of what is not. Because He, exalted, knew of the disbeliever, for example, that he will not believe, so faith became impossible (muḥāl) due to the impossibility of knowledge turning into ignorance. And God, exalted, is knowing of its impossibility, and the one who knows the impossibility of a thing does not will it necessarily. Also, if He willed it, then either it would occur, necessitating change (inqilāb), or it would not occur, necessitating His inability and deficiency in achieving His intention. And because from Him—i.e., from one who knows the impossibility of a thing—a preference-giving attribute (ṣifat tarjīḥ) for one of its two sides cannot be conceived, because one of them is impossible and the other is necessary, so there is no meaning to preferring by an attribute. And there is discussion in this because the non-faith of the disbeliever is willed by God despite it being necessary, and also it is refuted by what God knows to exist, like the faith of the believer, for one of its sides is necessary and the other is impossible, so there is no meaning to preferring by an attribute. And this, which is our doctrine, is supported by the consensus (ijmāʿ) of the predecessors and successors in ages and regions on their unrestricted statement: 'What God wills is, and what He does not will is not.' For this is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him), and the community received it with acceptance, so it is valid as a support. Rather, it may even be used as proof. And he explicitly stated the unrestrictedness to repel the misconception of restriction to His acts or to what is not from the voluntary acts of servants, as the Muʿtazila interpreted it. And this misconception is repelled by the fact that they used to utter their words in the context of glorifying God and exalting His status. And the first—i.e., 'What God wills is'—is evidence for the second—that He, exalted, is not willing of what is not—because it converts by contrapositive (ʿaks al-naqīḍ) to our saying: 'Everything that is not, God did not will.' And the second—I mean 'What He does not will is not'—is evidence for the first, by its conversion by that method to our saying: 'Everything that is, God has willed it.'
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God wills all things that exist and does not will what does not exist. It begins with a definition: God knows that the disbeliever will not believe, so faith is impossible because it would require God's knowledge to change into ignorance. Since God knows the impossibility of faith, He cannot will it—this is presented as necessary. A second argument: if God had willed faith for the disbeliever, it either would occur (necessitating a change in knowledge) or would not occur (implying God's inability). Third: from one who knows something is impossible, a preference-giving attribute cannot be conceived for one of its two sides, because one is impossible and the other necessary. The author then notes a discussion (فيه بحث): the non-faith of the disbeliever is willed by God despite being necessary, and this is refuted by God's knowledge of the believer's faith, which has one side necessary and the other impossible. After this, he supports the doctrine with consensus: the community's phrase 'What God wills is, what He does not will is not' is a decisive proof. He explains that this phrase is unrestricted to avoid the Muʿtazilite restriction to God's own acts or to non-voluntary acts. Finally, he shows the logical contrapositive: 'What God wills is' implies 'What is not, God did not will,' and 'What God does not will is not' implies 'What is, God has willed it.' This completes the proof.

Key Terms
إجماع
عكس النقيض
ما شاء الله كان
تقييد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The saying 'What God wills is' only applies to God's own actions and not to human actions.
Correction: The passage explicitly refutes this Mu'tazilite interpretation, stating that the saying is unrestricted and was used by the early community to glorify God's absolute sovereignty over all events.

Checkpoint
What logical operation does the author use to show that 'What God wills is' implies 'Everything that is not, God did not will'?
A) Syllogism
B) Contrapositive (ʿaks al-naqīḍ)
C) Induction
D) Analogy

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of divine will is supported by both rational argument and transmitted consensus, with logical coherence highlighted by contrapositive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'ما شاء الله كان', do you truly submit to God's will even in trials that shake your soul, or do you utter it as a mere habit? Will you pause today and say it with full conviction over the one thing you find hardest to accept?
?
The early community used this phrase to glorify God. In moments of anger or disappointment, do your words still reflect that glorification, or do they reveal a heart that questions God’s decree? Can you make a sincere commitment to only speak of what befalls you with this phrase, as an act of worship?
?
If everything that exists is willed by God, then your every success and failure is from Him. Does this truth fill you with humility and gratitude, or does it expose a hidden pride that resists total reliance on Him? How will you let this lesson reshape your attitude toward your own achievements and shortcomings starting today?

Good and Evil Defined by Sharia
The author defines good and evil as determined by Sharia, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Sunni theology, good and evil are determined by divine law (Sharia), not by human reason.

Core Idea
Good is what Sharia permits; evil is what it prohibits.

This lesson introduces the foundational Sunni position on moral evaluation: good and evil are not independent rational categories but are defined by divine command. The author explains that evil is whatever the Law prohibits, and good is its opposite—including the obligatory, recommended, and permissible. Even God's acts are always good.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the phrase 'al-qubḥ with us is what the Law has prohibited.' This is the core statement.
2
Identify the categories of good
The author lists that good includes the obligatory, recommended, and permissible. Also note that God's acts are always good.
3
Contrast with rationalist views
Reflect on how this definition differs from the Mu'tazilite view that good and evil are known by reason. This is a key sectarian difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الخامس : في الحسن والقبح ( في الحسن والقبح القبيح) عندنا ( ما نهى عنه شرعاً ) نهي تحريم أو تنزيه ) والحسن بخلافه ) أي ما لم ينه عنه شرعاً كالواجب والمندوب والمباح فإن المباح عند أكثر أصحابنا من قبيل الحسن وكفعل الله سبحانه وتعالى فإنه حسن أبدا قوله : (نهي تحريم أو تنزيه المنهي عنه نهي تنزيه يكون مكروهاً كراهة تنزيه وهو إلى الحل أقرب بمعنى أن تاركه يثاب عليه أدنى ثواب ولا يعاقب فاعله والمنهي عنه نهى تحريم حرام عند محمد رحمه الله، وأما عندهما فإما حرام أو قريب منه وهو المكروه كراهة التحريم ومعنى قربه منه أن فاعله لايدخل النار بفعله لكن يستحق محذوراً آخر كان يحرم الشفاعة لرفع الدرجة أو لعدم إدخال النار لكن حرماناً مؤقتاً.
The Fifth Objective: On al-ḥusn (good) and al-qubḥ (evil). 'Al-qubḥ (evil) with us is what the Law (sharʿ) has prohibited, be it a prohibition of tahrim (unlawfulness) or tanzih (disapproval). And al-ḥusn (good) is the opposite of that,' i.e., what the Law has not prohibited, such as the obligatory, recommended, and permissible. For the permissible according to most of our companions is of the category of good, as is the act of God—exalted and high—for it is always good. His saying: 'a prohibition of tahrim or tanzih.' What is prohibited by a tanzih prohibition is makruh (disliked) with a tanzih dislike, and it is closer to permissibility, meaning that the one who abandons it is rewarded with a minor reward, and the one who does it is not punished. What is prohibited by a tahrim prohibition is ḥarām (unlawful) according to Muḥammad (may God have mercy on him). According to the two [Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf?], it is either ḥarām or close to it, and it is the makruh tahrimī. The meaning of its closeness to ḥarām is that the doer does not enter the Fire due to his action, but he deserves some other deterrent, such as being deprived of intercession for raising his rank or for not entering the Fire, but a temporary deprivation.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage defines good and evil according to Sunni theology. The author states that evil (al-qubh) is what the Law (Sharia) has prohibited, whether the prohibition is one of tahrim (unlawfulness) or tanzih (disapproval). Good (al-husn) is the opposite, i.e., what the Law has not prohibited, including the obligatory, recommended, and permissible. The permissible is considered good according to most of the author's school (likely Hanafi). Even God's acts are always good. Then a note explains the difference between prohibition of tahrim and tanzih. Tanzih prohibition creates a makruh tanzihi, which is closer to halal: the one who avoids it gets a small reward, but the one who does it is not punished. Tahrim prohibition creates haram according to Imam Muhammad. According to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf?), it is either haram or close to it (makruh tahrimi). The closeness means the doer does not enter Hell for that act but deserves some other penalty, such as deprivation of intercession for raising his rank or temporary deprivation from Paradise.

Key Terms
الحسن
القبيح
الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'evil' is defined by rational judgment or social convention.
Correction: The text explicitly states that evil is defined solely by Sharia prohibition, not by reason or custom.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the definition of al-qubḥ (evil)?
A) Whatever reason deems bad.
B) Whatever the Law has prohibited.
C) Whatever causes harm.
D) Whatever is disliked by people.

Key Takeaway
Sunni theology defines good and evil by divine command, not by human reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If good and evil are defined only by Sharia, then what does that mean for the times you have judged an action as 'wrong' without consulting divine guidance? Are you willing to submit your own moral instincts to the authority of revelation, even when they conflict?
?
The passage says God's acts are always good. When you face trials or difficulties in your life that seem 'bad' to you, do you truly believe that they are good because they come from God? How can you train your heart to accept His decree with trust?
?
Consider a permissible (mubāḥ) action in your daily life—something you do without thinking. How can you transform even that into an act of worship by aligning your intention with Allah's command? In what areas of your life are you still living as if your own judgment determines right and wrong?

Categories of Prohibition: Tahrim and Tanzih
The author explains the two types of prohibition and their effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of Sharia prohibition—tahrim (unlawfulness) and tanzih (disapproval)—and their consequences for reward and punishment.

Core Idea
Prohibition can be either absolute (haram) or recommendatory (makruh), each with different consequences.

Building on the definition of evil as what is prohibited, this lesson delves into the two types of prohibition: tahrim (unlawfulness) and tanzih (disapproval). The author explains the legal and eschatological consequences of each, including the Hanafi difference of opinion on makruh tahrimi.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two prohibitions
Read the passage and identify the two categories: prohibition of tahrim and prohibition of tanzih. Note their Arabic terms.
2
Understand consequences for tanzih
The one who abandons a tanzih-prohibited act gets a minor reward; the one who does it is not punished. This is makruh tanzihi.
3
Understand consequences for tahrim
According to Imam Muhammad, it is haram. According to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), it is either haram or close to it (makruh tahrimi). The doer does not enter Hell but may face temporary deprivation.

The Ash'ari Definition of Good and Bad
Al-Jurjani defines good and bad as determined by divine command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school defines good and bad solely based on divine command, and the categories of prohibition (tahrim and tanzih).

Core Idea
Good and bad are determined by the Law, not by reason.

This lesson presents the core Ash'ari position on moral value: an act's goodness or badness is not intrinsic but depends on divine command. Al-Jurjani states this clearly in the passage below.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's words.
Pay attention to the brackets: they indicate the beginning of the objective.
2
Identify the two key definitions
Find where al-Jurjani defines 'bad' (qubh) and 'good' (husn). Notice that bad is defined by what is forbidden, good by what is not forbidden.
3
Note the categories of prohibition
The passage mentions two types of prohibition: nahy tahrim (prohibition of impermissibility) and nahy tanzih (prohibition of disapproval). Understand the difference.
This distinction is important for legal rulings.
4
Understand the permissibility of the permissible
The text says the permissible (mubah) is considered good by most Ash'aris. Reflect on why: it is not forbidden, so it falls under 'good'.
This shows that 'good' includes more than just obligatory acts.
5
Reflect on Allah's actions
The passage ends by stating that all of Allah's actions are good. This is because He cannot be subject to command.
This is a theological principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الخامس : في الحسن والقبح ] ( في الحسن والقبح القبيح عندنا ما نهى عنه شرعاً نهي تحريم أو تنزيه والحسن بخلافه أي ما لم ينه عنه شرعاً كالواجب والمندوب والمباح فإن المباح عند أكثر أصحابنا من قبيل الحسن وكفعل الله سبحانه وتعالى فإنه حسن أبدا )
The Fifth Objective: On Good and Bad. (Concerning good and bad, the bad according to us is what the Law has forbidden, a prohibition of prohibition or a prohibition of disapproval; and good is the opposite of that, i.e., what the Law has not forbidden, such as the obligatory, recommended, and permissible, for the permissible according to most of our companions is of the type of good, and like the action of Allah Almighty, for it is always good.)
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage defines the key terms of the chapter (husn and qubh) according to the Ash'ari school. It states that goodness and badness are determined solely by divine command (shar'), not by reason or intrinsic properties. The definition explicitly includes the distinction between prohibitions of tanzih (disapproval) and tahrim (prohibition), and clarifies that the permissible (mubah) is considered good. It also affirms that all of Allah's actions are good by consensus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris deny that good and bad have any meaning at all.
Correction: Ash'aris affirm that good and bad are real but defined by divine command: good is what is not forbidden, bad is what is forbidden.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari definition in this passage, what makes an act 'good'?
A) Intrinsic property of the act
B) Rational judgment
C) The Law not having forbidden it
D) Agreement with human nature

Key Takeaway
In the Ash'ari school, the moral value of an act is determined by whether the Law has forbidden it or not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered an action 'good' simply because your society or culture approves, while ignoring whether the Law has forbidden it? How will you train yourself to check your moral judgments against the Shari‘a, even when it contradicts popular opinion?
?
When you come across a clear divine prohibition that contradicts your personal reasoning or desire, which will you follow? What practical step will you take to submit your will to the Law, even when it feels difficult?
?
Think of an action you currently consider 'harmless' or 'permissible' based on your own judgment. Have you verified that the Law has not forbidden it? If you discovered it is actually disapproved or forbidden, would you abandon it? What would that change in your life?

Three Mu'tazilite Definitions of Evil
Jarjani presents three Mu'tazilite definitions of evil with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three Mu'tazilite definitions of evil (al-qabih) and the conditions that determine moral responsibility.

Core Idea
Evil is defined by the agent's ability, knowledge, and blameworthiness.

The Mu'tazila developed precise definitions of evil to ground moral responsibility. In this passage, Jarjani presents Abu al-Husayn's definition of al-qabih and adds two more, each refining the conditions under which an action is considered evil. These definitions clarify who can be blamed and why.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is dense; pay attention to the conditions of ability and knowledge.
2
Identify the three definitions
Find Abu al-Husayn's definition and the two that follow. Note how each definition builds on the concept of 'ability to know' and 'deserving blame'.
The definitions are nested; the second and third expand on the first.
3
Understand the conditions
Examine why ability excludes the coerced and knowledge excludes the unaware. Consider the example of the mountaintop disbeliever who can know God through reason.
This shows that potential knowledge suffices for moral responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأحسن ما نقل عنهم في العبارات الحدية قول أبي الحسين: القبيح ما ليس للمتمكن منه ومن العلم بحاله أن يفعله. اعتبر قيد التمكن احترازاً عن فعل العاجز والملجأ فإنه لا يوصف بقبح ولا حسن وقيد العلم ليخرج عنه المحرمات الصادرة عمن لم تبلغه دعوة نبي أو عمن هو قريب العهد بالإسلام واكتفى بالتمكن من العلم ليدخل فيه الكفر ممن في شاهق الجبل فإنه متمكن من العلم بالله تعالى بالدلائل العقلية. وأراد بقوله : ليس له أن يفعله أن الإقدام عليه لا يلائم عقل العقلاء ويتبعه أو يتبع هذا التعريف المذكور للقبيح تعريفان آخران له : أحدهما : أنه فعل يستحق الذم فاعله المتمكن منه ومن العلم بحاله، وذلك أنه لم يكن له أن يفعله . وثانيهما : أنه فعل هو على صفة تؤثر في استحقاق الذم إذ لو لم يكن كذلك لكان للقادر العالم به أن يفعله والذم : قول أو فعل أو ترك قول أو فعل ينبئ عن اتضاع حال الغير وانحطاط شأنه
And the best that has been reported from them in terms of definitions is the saying of Abu al-Husayn: 'The evil is that which it is not for the one who is able and knowing of its state to do.' He considered the condition of ability to exclude the action of the incapable and the coerced, for it is not described as evil or good; and the condition of knowledge to exclude from it the prohibited acts committed by one whom the call of a prophet has not reached, or by one who is new to Islam. And he was content with the ability to know so as to include in it the disbelief of one who is on a mountaintop, for he is able to know God Almighty by rational proofs. And he intended by his statement 'it is not for him to do it' that undertaking it is not compatible with the intellect of rational people. And this mentioned definition of the evil is followed by two other definitions for it: one of them is that it is an action whose doer, who is able and knowing of its state, deserves blame, because it was not for him to do it. And the second is that it is an action that is in a condition that effects deserving blame, for if it were not so, then the capable and knowing person would be permitted to do it. And blame is a statement, or an act, or the omission of a statement or act, that indicates the lowering of the state of another and the degradation of his rank.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage provides three formal definitions of al-qabih (the evil) as articulated by the Mu'tazila. First, the definition of Abu al-Husayn: 'The evil is that which it is not for one who is able (mutamakkin) and knowing of its state to do.' The commentary explains the conditions: 'ability' excludes the incapable and coerced (their actions are not described as evil), and 'knowledge' excludes acts done by those who have not heard the prophetic call or are new Muslims. However, 'ability to know' is enough to include disbelief even from one isolated on a mountain, as he can know God by rational proofs. The meaning of 'not for him to do' is that rational people would not find it reasonable. Then two additional definitions follow: (1) an action for which the able and knowing doer deserves blame because it was not for him to do it; (2) an action that has a quality that effects blameworthiness, otherwise the capable and knowing could do it. Finally, blame (dhamm) is defined as a word, act, or omission that indicates the belittlement of another. These definitions are meant to clarify the Mu'tazila concept of evil.

Key Terms
القبيح
الذم
التمكن
العلم بالحال
العاجز
الملجأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The condition 'ability' in the definition of evil refers only to actual ability at the moment of action.
Correction: It refers to potential ability (tamakkun), which includes the capacity to know and act, even if not currently able.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the condition 'al-tamakkun min al-ilm' in the definition of al-qabih?
A) To exclude the actions of those who are unaware of the divine command
B) To include the actions of those who have the potential to know
C) Both A and B
D) To limit the definition to voluntary actions only

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila define evil as an action that a capable and knowledgeable agent should not do, and for which they are blameworthy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a sin you have committed. Were you truly unable to know it was wrong, or did you have the ability to know? According to this definition of evil, are you still responsible? What does that mean for your repentance?
?
The Mu'tazila define blame as an act that belittles another. When you criticise someone, is your aim to belittle or to correct? Will you commit today to avoid blame (dhamm) and instead give sincere advice?
?
If someone has never heard of Islam, do you consider them evil for their disbelief? This passage says the condition of knowledge excludes them—until they have the ability to know. How does this change how you view others' beliefs and your own duty to convey the message?
