Defining Evil: Conditions of Ability and Knowledge
Sanusi defines evil with conditions of ability and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the primary definition of evil (al-qabih) and the role of the conditions of ability (al-tamakkun) and knowledge (al-ʿilm) in excluding certain acts from moral description.

Core Idea
An act is evil only when a capable and knowing agent ought not to do it.

Sanusi begins by laying down the central definition of evil in kalam: it is an act that a capable and knowledgeable agent should not perform. He carefully adds two conditions—ability and knowledge—to exclude the acts of the incapable, coerced, and ignorant from moral judgment. This lesson unpacks that definition and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Read the primary definition
Read the Arabic sentence from the beginning up to 'ليس له أن يفعله'. Notice how Sanusi defines evil in terms of the agent's capacity and knowledge.
The definition is: 'what the one who is able and knowledgeable of its condition is not permitted to do'.
2
Identify the two conditions
Look for the phrases 'قيد التمكن' and 'قيد العلم'. Understand what each condition excludes: inability/coercion and ignorance.
The condition of ability excludes the acts of the incapable and coerced. The condition of knowledge excludes those who have not received revelation or are new Muslims.
3
Reflect on the scope of knowledge
Consider why Sanusi says 'اكتفى بالتمكن من العلم' and gives the example of disbelief from a mountain peak. This shows that rational knowledge suffices for moral responsibility.
Even without prophetic revelation, one can know God through intellectual proofs.
4
Understand 'not permitted'
Sanusi clarifies that 'ليس له أن يفعله' means the act is not in harmony with reason. Reflect on how this relates to rational good and evil.
This connects to the idea that moral judgments are accessible to reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد الخامس : في الحسن والقبح للمتمكن منه ومن العلم بحاله أن يفعله اعتبر قيد التمكن احترازاً عن فعل العاجز والملجأ فإنه لا يوصف بقبح ولا حسن وقيد العلم ليخرج عنه المحرمات الصادرة عمن لم تبلغه دعوة نبي أو عمن هو قريب العهد بالإسلام واكتفى بالتمكن من العلم ليدخل فيه الكفر ممن في شاهق الجبل فإنه متمكن من العلم بالله تعالى بالدلائل العقلية. وأراد بقوله : ليس له أن يفعله أن الإقدام عليه لا يلائم عقل العقلاء ( ويتبعه ) أو يتبع هذا التعريف المذكور للقبيح تعريفان آخران له : أحدهما : ( أنه) فعل ( يستحق الذم فاعله المتمكن منه ومن العلم بحاله، وذلك أنه لم يكن له أن يفعله . ( و ) ثانيهما : ( أنه ( فعل هو ( على صفة تؤثر في استحقاق الذم إذ لو لم يكن كذلك لكان للقادر العالم به أن يفعله (والذم : قول أو فعل أو ترك قول أو فعل ينبئ عن اتضاع حال الغير وانحطاط شأنه . قوله : (اعتبر) قيد التمكن احترازاً عن فعل العاجز والملجأ اعترض عليه بأنه يصدق على بعض أفعالهما أنه ليس للمتمكن منه أن يفعله فلا يخرج بهذا القيد عن التعريف بل الظاهر أنه اعتبر قيد التمكن لئلا يخرج فعلهما عن التعريف لأن مآلهما أن فعلهما قد يكون قبيحاً . التفتازاني في شرح التنقيح وجوابه أن الشارح اعتبر شخصاً ما فعلاً وهو فعلهما المعصوم مثلاً ولا شك أنه ليس بقبيح فيجب إخراجه ثم الموصوف ،مقدور كما هو القاعدة في المشتقات التي لم يذكر معها موصوفاتها والتقدير هاهنا بقرينة قوله : أن يفعله ما ليس لفاعله المتمكن منه أن يفعله، نعم لو اعتبر نوع ما فعلاه يوجب شمول الحد له وعدم تقدير الموصوف المذكور إذ لا شك في قبحه وهو مغاط ما ذكره في التلويح .
The Sixth Observation Point - The Fifth Objective: On Good and Evil. For the one who is able (al-mutamakkin) and knowledgeable of its condition to do it, he considered the condition of ability to avoid the act of the incapable and the coerced, for it is not described as evil nor good. And the condition of knowledge to exclude from it the forbidden acts that occur from one whom the call of a prophet has not reached, or from one who is new in Islam. And he sufficed with the ability to knowledge to include disbelief from one who is on a mountain peak, for he is able to know Allah Almighty through intellectual proofs. And he meant by his saying: 'He is not permitted to do it' that embarking upon it is not in harmony with the intellect of rational people. (And follows it) Or this mentioned definition of evil is followed by two other definitions of it: One of them: (that it is) an act (for which the doer, who is able and knowledgeable of its condition, deserves blame, because he should not have done it). And (the second): (that it is) an act (that is) (on a quality that effects deservedness of blame, because if it were not so, then the capable and knowledgeable one would be permitted to do it). (And blame: is a saying, or an act, or the omission of a saying or act that indicates the lowly state and degraded standing of the other). His saying: ('Considered' the condition of ability to avoid the act of the incapable and coerced) it was objected that it applies to some of their acts that it is not for the one able to do it, so it does not exclude by this condition from the definition. Rather, it is apparent that he considered the condition of ability lest their act be excluded from the definition, because their ultimate outcome is that their act may be evil. Al-Taftazani in Sharh al-Tanqih and his answer is that the commentator considered a particular individual's act, and it is their act, like the infallible, and there is no doubt that it is not evil, so it must be excluded. Then the described is the one who has power, as is the rule for derived nouns when their described nouns are not mentioned, and the implicit noun here is, by the context of his saying: 'what is not for its doer who is able to do it'. Yes, if he considered the type of what they did, it would necessitate the inclusion of the definition and the non-implication of the mentioned described noun, because there is no doubt in its evilness, and it is the purport of what he mentioned in al-Talwih.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'evil' (al-qabih) in the context of rational and revealed good and evil. The author begins by stating that an act is evil if the agent is able (mutamakkin) to know its condition and yet does it. Two conditions are introduced: al-tamakkun (ability) and al-ʿilm (knowledge). The condition of ability excludes the acts of the incapable and the coerced from being described as evil. The condition of knowledge excludes those who are ignorant of the prohibition, such as those who have not received a prophet's call or new Muslims. The definition includes disbelief from someone on a remote mountain peak because they are able to know God through intellectual proofs. The author then clarifies that 'he is not permitted to do it' means that committing the act is not compatible with reason. Two alternative definitions are given: (1) an act whose doer, being able and knowledgeable, deserves blame for it; (2) an act that has a quality that causes deservedness of blame, otherwise a capable and knowledgeable agent would be allowed to do it. Blame is defined as speech, act, or omission that indicates the lowly status of another. Following this, a detailed note critiques the condition of ability: it is argued that some acts of the incapable and coerced would still be considered evil by this definition if the condition is meant to exclude them. The commentator (likely al-Sharif al-Jurjani) responds that the original author (al-Iji) considered a specific individual's act (e.g., the infallible) rather than the type of act, and thus exclusion is valid. If the type of the act were considered, it would be evil, but that is not the intended meaning.

Key Terms

القبح
التمكن
العلم
العاجز
الملجأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that evil acts are defined solely by divine command, not by rational criteria.
Correction: Sanusi's definition shows that evil is rationally determined: an act is evil if a capable and knowledgeable agent should not do it, and reason can discern that.

Checkpoint
Which of the following acts is excluded from being described as evil according to the condition of knowledge?
A) The act of a person who is physically forced to do something.
B) The act of a person who has never heard a prophet's call.
C) The act of a person who is fully aware of the prohibition.
D) The act of a person who is able but chooses to ignore.

Key Takeaway
Evil is defined rationally as an act that a capable and knowledgeable agent should not perform, and two conditions—ability and knowledge—ensure fairness in moral judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused yourself from a moral duty because you felt unable or coerced? Consider: are you truly powerless, or are you avoiding the condition of ability? What step will you take today to reclaim your agency before Allah?
?
The person on the mountain peak is held accountable because they can know God through reason. Do you rely on excuses of ignorance when truth is accessible to you? What prevents you from seeking knowledge, and will you commit to studying one rational proof for Allah's existence this week?
?
Sanusi says evil acts are incompatible with sound reason. When you sin, do you acknowledge that your intellect condemns the act, or do you rationalize it? Will you confront that inner rational judgment today and repent from a specific sin you have been justifying?

Two Alternative Definitions of Evil and the Incapable Actor Problem
Sanusi gives two alternative definitions and resolves an objection about the condition of ability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two alternative definitions of evil and how a potential objection about the acts of the incapable is resolved.

Core Idea
Evil can also be defined by deservedness of blame or by a quality that merits blame.

After presenting the primary definition, Sanusi offers two alternative formulations of evil: one emphasizing deserved blame, another focusing on the quality of the act. He then addresses a subtle objection regarding the condition of ability: if we consider the type of act rather than the specific agent, acts of the incapable could still be considered evil. The commentary clarifies this point.

Guided Path
1
Read the two alternative definitions
Read the Arabic from 'ويتبعه' to 'أن يفعله'. Identify the two definitions and note how each relates to blame.
The first alternative: 'فعل يستحق الذم فاعله المتمكن منه ومن العلم بحاله'. The second: 'فعل هو على صفة تؤثر في استحقاق الذم'.
2
Understand the definition of blame
Sanusi defines blame (al-dhamm) as speech or action indicating the lowly state of another. This clarifies the consequence of evil.
Blame is a social-linguistic concept.
3
Analyze the objection
Read the passage starting from 'اعترض عليه بأنه يصدق على بعض أفعالهما...' The objection argues that the condition of ability does not fully exclude acts of the incapable because some of their acts would still be considered evil if taken as types.
The objection concerns whether the definition applies to the specific act (of the incapable) or the type of act.
4
Follow the response
The commentator responds that the original author considered a specific individual's act (e.g., an infallible person) when applying the condition, not the act-type. Thus, the exclusion holds. If the type were considered, it would be evil, but that is not the intended application.
This distinction is important for the precise scope of the definition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an act is evil, any similar act performed by anyone, even the incapable, is also evil.
Correction: Evil is defined relative to the agent's capacity. An act of the same type performed by an incapable person is not evil because they lack ability.

Checkpoint
According to the objection discussed in the passage, why might the condition of ability be considered problematic?
A) Because it excludes too many acts from being considered evil.
B) Because some acts of the incapable could still be considered evil if the type of act is considered.
C) Because it only applies to acts of the incapable but not the coerced.
D) Because it contradicts the second definition of evil.

Key Takeaway
Evil can be understood through deserved blame or blame-deserving qualities, and the condition of ability must be applied to the specific agent, not the act-type, to maintain consistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you blamed someone severely for a mistake. Did you first consider whether they had the ability and knowledge to act otherwise? How would applying this lesson's condition of ability change your judgment? Will you commit to holding back condemnation until you assess the other's capacity?
?
When you blame yourself for a past sin, are you assuming full ability and knowledge? Or do you honestly acknowledge your limitations? How might a correct understanding of blame—as an indication of lowly state, not a tool for self-flagellation—transform your self-talk starting today?
?
If blame is a communicative act indicating lowly standing, do you use blame to genuinely correct or to elevate yourself? Before you blame anyone today, ask yourself: Is this blame deserved, and am I saying it to help or to harm? Will you pause before every word of blame to check your intention?

The Objection of Ugliness and the Reply
Objection and reply about ugliness subsisting in non-existent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that ugliness would require a real attribute in the non-existent, by distinguishing the intellect's judgment from a pre-existing attribute.

Core Idea
Ugliness is a rational judgment, not a pre-existing attribute.

This passage presents a classic objection against intrinsic moral qualities in acts. The opponents argue that if ugliness were inherent, it would have to exist before the act, leading to absurdities. The author responds by clarifying that ugliness is a judgment made by the intellect when the act occurs, not a separate entity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage which presents the objection. Notice the two consequences drawn: either a real attribute exists in the non-existent, or the effect precedes its cause.
The objection is structured as a conditional: if ugliness were intrinsic, then...
2
Identify the two consequences
The objection claims that intrinsic ugliness would lead to one of two absurdities. Identify each one and understand why they are considered problematic in the context of kalam.
The first consequence involves a real attribute subsisting in non-existence; the second involves a chronological impossibility.
3
Understand the author's reply
Read the reply starting with 'قلنا'. The author denies the premise that ugliness is obtained before the act. Instead, he explains that the intellect judges the act as ugly when it occurs, and this judgment itself deters the agent.
Pay attention to the phrase 'بل يحكم العقل' – this shift to rational judgment is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قبحه حاصل قبل وجوده لم يكن كذلك، ويلزم حينئذ قيام الصفة الحقيقية بالمعدوم لأن مقتضى القبح صفة وجودية. وقد يقال: لو كان القبح ذاتياً لزم تقدم المعلول على علته لأن قبيح الفعل حاصل قبله لما عرفت وعلته إما ذات الفعل وصفته وليس شيء منهما حاصلا قبله. قلنا: لا نسلم أن القبح أو علته حاصل قبل الفعل بل يحكم العقل باتصافه بالقبح وبما يقتضيه إذا حصل، وهذا الحكم هو المانع من فعله والإقدام عليه لاتصافه بالقبح أو بما يقتضيه، على أن القدماء منهم زعموا أن الذوات ثابتة متقررة في الأزل فيصح عندهم اتصافها بالصفات الثبوتية.
Its ugliness is obtained before its existence is not so, and it necessitates then the subsistence of the real attribute in the non-existent because the implication of ugliness is an existential attribute. And it may be said: if ugliness were intrinsic, it would necessitate the priority of the effect over its cause because the ugliness of the act is obtained before it, as you have known, and its cause is either the essence of the act and its attribute, and none of them is obtained before it. We said: we do not concede that ugliness or its cause is obtained before the act, rather the intellect judges that it is described by ugliness and by what it necessitates when it occurs, and this judgment is the deterrent from doing it and advancing upon it because of its being described by ugliness or by what it necessitates. Moreover, the early ones among them claimed that essences are fixed and established in pre-eternity, so it is valid according to them that they be described by positive attributes.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the opponents (likely the Mu'tazila) about the concept of ugliness (qubh) being intrinsic to acts. The objection states that if ugliness were intrinsic, then it would have to exist before the act, leading to either a real attribute subsisting in a non-existent or the effect preceding its cause. The author (al-Jurjani in his commentary) responds by denying that ugliness or its cause is obtained before the act; rather, the intellect judges the act to be ugly when it occurs, and this judgment itself is the deterrent, not some pre-existing attribute. He also cites the older Mu'tazila who held that essences are fixed in pre-eternity, so they could allow attributes to subsist in non-existents.

Key Terms
القبح
الصفة الحقيقية
المعدوم
الذوات الثابتة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that ugliness is a real quality that exists independently of the act, like a physical property.
Correction: The author clarifies that ugliness is a rational judgment, not a separately existing attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the author's response to the objection?
A) Ugliness exists before the act.
B) The intellect's judgment is the deterrent, not a pre-existing attribute.
C) Ugliness is a positive attribute subsisting in the non-existent.
D) The act itself is the cause of ugliness.

Key Takeaway
The intellect's judgment of ugliness deters from an act, not a pre-existing attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you refrain from an action because you consider it ugly or wrong, do you believe there is a fixed 'ugliness' in the act itself that repels you, or is it your mind's judgment based on your moral framework? How does this lesson challenge your usual understanding?
?
Think of a time you felt drawn to an action you knew was wrong. According to this passage, what truly deters you—an external quality of the act or your own rational judgment? What would it mean to take full ownership of that judgment rather than blaming the act?
?
The early theologians held that essences are fixed in pre-eternity. Do you sometimes treat certain wrongs as timelessly evil, as if they were always wrong? How does this lesson help you see that moral value might be more closely tied to how we, as rational beings, judge actions in context?

The Permissibility of Imposing the Impossible: Ash'ari and Mu'tazila Views
Defines the permissibility of imposing the impossible and records the Mu'tazila objection using analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on the permissibility of imposing an impossible burden and the Mu'tazila objection based on rational ugliness.

Core Idea
God's absolute sovereignty permits what human reason may call ugly.

This lesson introduces a key Ash'ari doctrine: that nothing is inherently good or evil for God, so He may command what is impossible. The Mu'tazila, however, argue by analogy with human judges that such commands are rationally ugly. We will read the passage that states both positions and examines the examples given.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the structure: the author first states the Ash'ari view, then the Mu'tazila objection.
2
Identify the Ash'ari principle
The passage begins by referencing a previous principle: nothing is obligatory for God and nothing is ugly from Him. This is the foundation for the permissibility.
This principle is key to understanding Ash'ari theology.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazila analogy
The Mu'tazila object using an analogy from human experience (shāhid). They argue that commanding an impossible act is considered foolish and ugly in human judgment.
Note the shift from divine to human reasoning.
4
Examine the examples
The passage gives three examples: a blind person dotting Qur'ans, a paralyzed person walking far, and a slave flying to the sky. These illustrate the ugliness in the witnessed world.
These are physically impossible, not logically contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السادس - المقصد السابع : جواز تكليف ما لا يطاق أنه ( تكليف ما لا يطاق جائز عندنا لما قدمنا آنفاً ) في المقصد السادس ( من لا يجب عليه شيء ولا يقبح منه شيء إذ يفعل ما يشاء ويحكم ما يريد لا معقب ومنعه المعتزلة لقبحه عقلا كما في الشاهد (فإن من كلف الأعمى نقط المصاحف والزمن المشي إلى أقاصي البلاد وعبده الطيران إلى السماء عد سفيهاً، وقبح ذلك في بداية العقول وكان كأمر الجماد الذي لا شك في كونه سفها. )
The Sixth Observation - The Seventh Purpose: The Permissibility of Imposing What Cannot Be Borne. That (imposing what cannot be borne is permissible according to us, due to what we have just presented) in the Sixth Purpose (that nothing is obligatory upon Him and nothing is ugly from Him, for He does what He wills and decrees what He desires, with no one overruling His decree). And the Mu'tazila prevented it because of its intellectual ugliness, as in the witnessed (for if someone were to impose on a blind person the dotting of Qur'ans, on a paralyzed person walking to the farthest lands, and on his slave flying to the sky, he would be deemed foolish, and that ugliness is in the beginning of intellects, and it would be like commanding a lifeless object, which is undoubtedly foolish).
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage defines the topic of imposing an impossible burden and states the Ash'ari position that it is permissible. The author begins by referencing the previous discussion that nothing is obligatory for God and nothing is ugly from Him because He acts absolutely. He then identifies the Mu'tazila objection, which relies on an analogy with human judges: in human experience, commanding an impossible act is considered foolish. The Mu'tazila extend this to God. The passage ends by giving examples (blind person dotting Qur'ans, etc.) to illustrate the ugliness in human judging, thereby setting up the conflict between the Ash'ari principle of divine sovereignty and the Mu'tazila ethical rationalism.

Key Terms

جائز
قبح
المعتزلة
الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'impossible burden' only refers to logically impossible acts.
Correction: The examples given show it also includes physically or practically impossible acts, not just logical contradictions.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, why is imposing an impossible burden considered ugly?
A) It contradicts God's mercy.
B) It is intellectually ugly based on human analogy.
C) It is impossible for God to command anything evil.
D) It is not found in the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position affirms divine sovereignty, while the Mu'tazila object using rational analogy from human experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that a command of Allah is 'too much' or 'unfair' based on your own sense of reason? How does this lesson challenge that inner judgment? Will you repent from that attitude today?
?
The Mu'tazila saw no difference between human and divine standards of ugliness. In your life, where have you unconsciously placed God under the same moral expectations you hold for people? What would it cost you to truly believe that 'nothing is ugly from Him'?
?
If God imposed something on you that you consider impossible—like complete patience in a tragedy or total reliance when you have no resources—would your first reaction be submission or objection? What does your answer reveal about your heart's view of divine sovereignty?

The Name is Other than the Naming
Defines tasmiya as the act of naming and distinguishes it from ism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the name (ism) and the act of naming (tasmiya) in Islamic theology, and why they are considered different entities.

Core Idea
The name is not the naming; the act is distinct from the word.

This lesson introduces a foundational distinction in the study of God's names. Before discussing the divine attributes themselves, the author clarifies the relationship between the name (ism) and the naming (tasmiya). This seemingly simple distinction has important implications for how we understand language and reality in theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's main points.
Notice how the passage includes both the main text and marginal commentary.
2
Identify the two proofs for the distinction
The passage gives two arguments: (1) intuition (badīha) shows that assigning a name is different from the name itself; (2) tasmiya is an act that occurs in time and is past, while the name is not limited to the past.
3
Understand the opposing view and its dismissal
Some scholars claimed tasmiya is identical to the names (the spoken words). The author dismisses this as an unnecessary invention without linguistic basis.
The marginal note adds that 'other' here means 'conventional other' (al-ghayr al-iṣṭilāḥī), which is a technical term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السابع في أسماء الله تعالى وبه تنتهي مباحث الإلهيات وفيه مقاصد المقصد الأول: الاسم غير التسمية. الاسم غير التسمية لأنها تخصيص الاسم ووضعه للشيء ولا شك أنه أي تخصيص الاسم بشيء مغاير له أي للاسم كما تشهد به البديهة وأيضا التسمية فعل الواضع وأنه منقض فيما مضى من الزمان وليس الاسم كذلك وذهب بعضهم إلى أن التسمية عين الأقوال الدالة التي هي الأسماء كما سيرد عليك ولم يلتفت إليه المصنف. قوله: (وأيضاً التسمية فعل الواضع) لأنها تخصيص الاسم بمعنى وقد يراد بها ذكر الشيء باسمه فيكون فعل المتكلم. قوله: (ولم يلتفت إليه المصنف) لأنه لا يصح بحسب اللغة إحداث اصطلاح جديد بلا ضرورة مما لا وجه له. قوله: (ولا شك أنها أي تلك النسبة غيره) فيه بحث ظاهر لأن سياق كلامه يدل على أن المراد بالغير هو الغير الاصطلاحي وقد سبق أنه من أقسام الموجود وأن النسبة ليست منها عند أهل السنة فليست هي ولا ما يشتمل عليها غير بالمعنى الاصطلاحي.
The seventh observation on the names of God Almighty, and with it the discussions of theology (al-ilāhiyyāt) conclude, and it contains purposes. The first purpose: the name is other than the naming. The name is other than the naming because it (the naming) is the assignment and placement of the name for a thing, and there is no doubt that it—i.e., the assignment of the name to something—is different from it—i.e., the name—as intuition (al-badīha) witnesses. And also, the naming is the act of the assigner and it is past in time that has elapsed, while the name is not like that. And some have held that the naming is identical to the indicative words that are the names, as will come to you, and the author paid no attention to it. His saying: 'And also, the naming is the act of the assigner' because it is the assignment of the name in a sense, and sometimes by it is meant the mention of a thing by its name, so it would be the act of the speaker. His saying: 'And the author paid no attention to it' because it is not correct according to language to invent a new convention without necessity, which has no basis. His saying: 'And there is no doubt that it—i.e., that relation—is other' there is apparent discussion in it because the context of his speech indicates that what is meant by 'other' is the conventional 'other' (al-ghayr al-iṣṭilāḥī), and it has preceded that it is among the divisions of the existent, and that the relation is not among them according to the People of the Sunnah, so neither it nor what it includes is 'other' in the conventional sense.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the distinction between 'ism' (name) and 'tasmiya' (naming). It begins by stating that the name is other than the naming. The reason given is that tasmiya is the act of assigning a name to a thing, which is clearly different from the name itself, as intuition attests. Additionally, tasmiya is an action of the assigner that is past and completed, while the name is not confined to the past. The author then notes that some have claimed tasmiya is identical to the indicative words (the names) themselves, but the commentator dismisses this, saying the author paid no attention to it. The marginal notes then explain specific phrases: first, that 'tasmiya' can also mean mentioning a thing by its name, which is the act of the speaker, and second, that inventing a new linguistic convention without necessity is invalid. Finally, a note discusses the phrase 'that relation is other,' clarifying that 'other' here means conventional other (a term from logic/metaphysics), and since relations are not considered existent entities according to the Sunnis, the relation is not 'other' in that technical sense.

Key Terms
الاسم
التسمية
الغير الاصطلاحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the name and the naming are identical because both derive from the same root and are closely related in meaning.
Correction: The author argues they are distinct: naming is an act (assigning or mentioning), while the name is the word itself. Intuition and the temporal nature of the act support this distinction.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the name is other than the naming?
A) Because the name is eternal and the naming is temporal.
B) Because the naming is the act of assigning the name, which is distinct from the name itself.
C) Because the name is spoken and the naming is written.
D) Because the naming is a divine act and the name is human.

Key Takeaway
The name and the naming are distinct: naming is an act, while the name is the word.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah' or 'Ar-Rahman', do you realize that your tongue is performing an act (tasmiya) that is separate from the name itself? How does that awareness change the weight of each utterance in your heart?
?
If the names of God are distinct from the act of calling upon Him, then what does it mean to truly 'remember' a name? Is it possible that you have been reciting His names without ever engaging in the act of sincere tasmiya—a conscious assignment of His names to His majesty in your mind?
?
The author dismisses equating the name with the naming as an unnecessary innovation. Examine your own worship: have you ever confused the label with the reality, treating the name of God as if the mere word holds power apart from the One it signifies? Will you now resolve to pause before each name and admit the distinction—that your act of speaking is not the same as the reality of His name?

Al-Ash'ari's Classification of Names
Al-Ash'ari's threefold classification of names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's three categories of how a name relates to the named: identical, other, and neither identical nor other.

Core Idea
Not all names are the same; they relate to the named in different ways.

The classical debate about whether the name (ism) is the same as the named (musamma) begins by clarifying the real point of contention: it is not about the literal word but about its meaning. Al-Ash'ari provides a fundamental typology that distinguishes between names that are identical to the essence, names that indicate relations, and names that indicate real attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage from 'وقد اشتهر الخلاف' to 'كذا الحال في الذات المأخوذة مع تلك الصفة' along with its literal translation. Notice the flow from clarifying the dispute to al-Ash'ari's threefold classification.
2
Identify al-Ash'ari's three categories
Find and list the three types of names according to al-Ash'ari: (1) identical to the named (e.g., 'Allah'), (2) other than the named (e.g., 'al-Khaliq', 'al-Raziq'), and (3) neither identical nor other (e.g., 'al-'Alim', 'al-Qadir').
Notice that each category corresponds to a different kind of meaning: essence alone, relation, or real attribute.
3
Reflect on the underlying principle
Consider why al-Ash'ari makes this distinction. The passage stresses that the dispute is about the meaning (madlool) of the name, not the physical word. How does this classification help clarify the theological discussion?
Al-Ash'ari's categorization is grounded in his doctrine of attributes: real attributes are neither identical to the essence nor other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اشتهر الخلاف في أن الاسم هل هو نفس المسمى أو غيره ؟ ولا يشك عاقل في أنه ليس النزاع في لفظه ف ر س أنه هل هو نفس الحيوان المخصوص أو غيره فإن هذا مما لا يشتبه على أحد بل النزاع في مدلول الاسم هو الذات من حيث هي هي أم هو الذات باعتبار أمر صادق عليه عارض له ينبئ عنه ، فلذلك قال الشيخ أبو الحسن الأشعري : قد يكون الاسم أي مدلوله عين المسمى أي ذاته من حيث هي نحو الله فإنه اسم علم للذات من غير اعتبار معنى فيه وقد يكون غيره نحو الخالق والرازق مما يدل على نسبته إلى غيره ولا شك أنها أي تلك النسبة غيره وقد يكون لا هو ولا غيره كالعليم والقدير مما يدل على صفة حقيقية قائمة بذاته ومن مذهبه أنها أي الصفة الحقيقية القائمة بذاته لا هو ولا غيره كما مر فكذا الحال في الذات المأخوذة مع تلك الصفة قال الآمدي : اتفق العقلاء على المغايرة بين التسمية والمسمى وذهب أكثر أصحابنا إلى أن التسمية هي نفس الأقوال الدالة وأن الاسم هو نفس المدلول، ثم اختلف هؤلاء فذهب ابن فورك وغيره إلى أن كل اسم هو المسمى بعينه فقولك : الله قول دال على اسم هو المسمى وكذا قولك : عالم وخالق فإنه يدل على الرب الموصوف بكونه عالماً وخالقا . وقال بعضهم: من الأسماء ما هو عين كالموجود ،والذات، ومنها ما هو غير كالخالق، فإن المسمى ذاته والاسم هو نفس الخلق وخلقه غير ذاته، ومنها ما ليس عيناً ولا غيراً كالعالم فإن المسمى ذاته والاسم علمه الذي ليس عين ذاته ولا غيرها. وذهبت المعتزلة إلى أن الاسم هو التسمية، ووافقهم على ذلك بعض المتأخرين من أصحابنا، وذهب الأستاذ أبو نصر بن أيوب إلى أن لفظ الاسم مشترك بين التسمية والمسمى فيطلق على كل منهما ويفهم المقصود بحسب القرائن، ولا يخفى عليك أن النزاع على قول أبي نصر إنما هو في لفظة اسم وأنها تطلق على الألفاظ فيكون التسمية بالمعنى المذكور لا بمعنى فعل الواضع أو تطلق على مدلولاتها
And the dispute has become famous about whether the name is identical to the named or other than it. No rational person doubts that the dispute is not about the word 'f-r-s' as to whether it is the specific animal or something else, for this is not ambiguous to anyone. Rather, the dispute is about the meaning of the name: is it the essence insofar as it is, or is it the essence by virtue of an attribute that is true of it, accidental to it, and indicates it? Therefore, Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said: The name—i.e., its meaning—may be identical to the named—i.e., its essence insofar as it is—as in 'Allah,' for it is a proper name for the essence without considering any meaning in it; and it may be other than it, as in 'al-Khaliq' and 'al-Raziq,' which indicate a relation to something else, and there is no doubt that it—i.e., that relation—is other; and it may be neither identical nor other, as in 'al-'Alim' and 'al-Qadir,' which indicate a real attribute subsisting in the essence, and it follows from his doctrine that it—i.e., the real attribute subsisting in the essence—is neither identical nor other, as has passed, and so likewise the situation is with the essence taken along with that attribute. Al-Amidi said: The rational people have agreed on the distinction between naming and the named, and most of our companions hold that the naming is identical to the indicative words and that the name is identical to the meaning. Then these differed: Ibn Furak and others held that every name is exactly the named; thus your saying 'Allah' is a statement indicating a name that is the named, and likewise your saying 'alim' and 'khaliq' because it indicates the Lord described as being knowing and creating. And some said: Among names, some are identical, like 'al-mawjud' and 'al-dhat'; some are other, like 'al-Khaliq,' for the named is His essence and the name is the act of creation itself, and His creation is other than His essence; and some are neither identical nor other, like 'al-'Alim,' for the named is His essence and the name is His knowledge, which is neither identical to His essence nor other than it. The Mu'tazila held that the name is the naming, and some later ones among our companions agreed with them. And the master Abu Nasr ibn Ayyub held that the word 'ism' is equivocal between naming and the named, so it is applied to each and the intended meaning is understood according to context. It is not hidden from you that the dispute according to Abu Nasr's view is only about the word 'ism' and whether it is applied to utterances—in which case naming is in the mentioned sense, not in the sense of the act of the assigner—or to their meanings.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the well-known dispute about the relationship between the name (ism) and the named (musamma). It clarifies that the dispute is not about the linguistic word itself but about its meaning (madlool). Al-Ash'ari is then cited, providing a threefold classification: some names are identical to the named (like 'Allah'), some are other (like 'al-Khaliq' indicating a relation), and some are neither identical nor other (like 'al-'Alim' indicating a real attribute). This classification is based on whether the name indicates the essence alone, a relation, or a real attribute. The passage then moves to al-Amidi, who reports that most Ash'aris hold that tasmiya is the indicative words and ism is the meaning. Among them, Ibn Furak says every name is the named, another group distinguishes types (identical, other, neither), the Mu'tazila identify ism with tasmiya, and Abu Nasr says the word is equivocal. The passage ends by noting that according to Abu Nasr, the dispute is merely verbal about the word 'ism'.

Key Terms
الاسم
المسمى
المدلول
الذات
الصفة الحقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute is about whether the word 'Allah' is literally God.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the dispute is about the meaning (madlool) of the name, not the physical word or utterance.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, which of the following names is classified as 'neither identical nor other'?
A) Allah
B) al-Khaliq
C) al-'Alim
D) al-Raziq

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari distinguishes names by whether they indicate essence alone, a relation, or a real attribute, leading to three types of relationship between name and named.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as al-Khaliq, do you realize that this name points to His relationship with creation, not His essence? How does this distinction make you more mindful of His absolute transcendence and your own dependence?
?
Al-Ash'ari teaches that names like al-'Alim and al-Qadir indicate real attributes that are neither identical nor other than Allah. Does this truth humble you, knowing that His knowledge and power are truly His, beyond your full comprehension? How will you let this shape your reverence when you utter these names?
?
The passage insists the dispute is about meaning, not mere words. Have you ever argued over religious terms while missing the deeper reality they signify? How will you train yourself to seek meanings first, especially when speaking about Allah?

Ibn Furak and the Differentiation of Names
Ibn Furak and the differentiation of names among Ash'aris.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different positions among Ash'ari scholars regarding whether every name is the named or only some.

Core Idea
Ibn Furak says all names are the named; others differentiate.

After al-Ash'ari, his followers debated the precise relationship. Al-Amidi reports that most Ash'aris distinguish between naming (tasmiya) as the words and name (ism) as the meaning. Then Ibn Furak claims every name is exactly the named, while others offer a nuanced classification of some names being identical, some other, and some neither.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Ash'ari views
Read from 'قال الآمدي : اتفق العقلاء' to 'ولا غيراً كالعالم فإن المسمى ذاته والاسم علمه' along with its translation. Pay attention to how al-Amidi sets the stage, then Ibn Furak's view, and then the alternative view.
2
Contrast Ibn Furak's view with the others
Ibn Furak holds that every name is exactly the named. The other group, however, says some names are identical (like 'al-mawjud'), some are other (like 'al-Khaliq'), and some are neither (like 'al-'Alim'). Note how each handles the examples.
Ibn Furak's position simplifies: the name 'al-Khaliq' indicates the Lord described as creating, so the meaning is the Lord himself, not the act of creation.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why these different positions matter. How does Ibn Furak's view avoid the distinction between essence and attributes? How does the alternative view preserve al-Ash'ari's original threefold classification?
The alternative view closely mirrors al-Ash'ari's own typology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اشتهر الخلاف في أن الاسم هل هو نفس المسمى أو غيره ؟ ولا يشك عاقل في أنه ليس النزاع في لفظه ف ر س أنه هل هو نفس الحيوان المخصوص أو غيره فإن هذا مما لا يشتبه على أحد بل النزاع في مدلول الاسم هو الذات من حيث هي هي أم هو الذات باعتبار أمر صادق عليه عارض له ينبئ عنه ، فلذلك قال الشيخ أبو الحسن الأشعري : قد يكون الاسم أي مدلوله عين المسمى أي ذاته من حيث هي نحو الله فإنه اسم علم للذات من غير اعتبار معنى فيه وقد يكون غيره نحو الخالق والرازق مما يدل على نسبته إلى غيره ولا شك أنها أي تلك النسبة غيره وقد يكون لا هو ولا غيره كالعليم والقدير مما يدل على صفة حقيقية قائمة بذاته ومن مذهبه أنها أي الصفة الحقيقية القائمة بذاته لا هو ولا غيره كما مر فكذا الحال في الذات المأخوذة مع تلك الصفة قال الآمدي : اتفق العقلاء على المغايرة بين التسمية والمسمى وذهب أكثر أصحابنا إلى أن التسمية هي نفس الأقوال الدالة وأن الاسم هو نفس المدلول، ثم اختلف هؤلاء فذهب ابن فورك وغيره إلى أن كل اسم هو المسمى بعينه فقولك : الله قول دال على اسم هو المسمى وكذا قولك : عالم وخالق فإنه يدل على الرب الموصوف بكونه عالماً وخالقا . وقال بعضهم: من الأسماء ما هو عين كالموجود ،والذات، ومنها ما هو غير كالخالق، فإن المسمى ذاته والاسم هو نفس الخلق وخلقه غير ذاته، ومنها ما ليس عيناً ولا غيراً كالعالم فإن المسمى ذاته والاسم علمه الذي ليس عين ذاته ولا غيرها. وذهبت المعتزلة إلى أن الاسم هو التسمية، ووافقهم على ذلك بعض المتأخرين من أصحابنا، وذهب الأستاذ أبو نصر بن أيوب إلى أن لفظ الاسم مشترك بين التسمية والمسمى فيطلق على كل منهما ويفهم المقصود بحسب القرائن، ولا يخفى عليك أن النزاع على قول أبي نصر إنما هو في لفظة اسم وأنها تطلق على الألفاظ فيكون التسمية بالمعنى المذكور لا بمعنى فعل الواضع أو تطلق على مدلولاتها
And the dispute has become famous about whether the name is identical to the named or other than it. No rational person doubts that the dispute is not about the word 'f-r-s' as to whether it is the specific animal or something else, for this is not ambiguous to anyone. Rather, the dispute is about the meaning of the name: is it the essence insofar as it is, or is it the essence by virtue of an attribute that is true of it, accidental to it, and indicates it? Therefore, Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said: The name—i.e., its meaning—may be identical to the named—i.e., its essence insofar as it is—as in 'Allah,' for it is a proper name for the essence without considering any meaning in it; and it may be other than it, as in 'al-Khaliq' and 'al-Raziq,' which indicate a relation to something else, and there is no doubt that it—i.e., that relation—is other; and it may be neither identical nor other, as in 'al-'Alim' and 'al-Qadir,' which indicate a real attribute subsisting in the essence, and it follows from his doctrine that it—i.e., the real attribute subsisting in the essence—is neither identical nor other, as has passed, and so likewise the situation is with the essence taken along with that attribute. Al-Amidi said: The rational people have agreed on the distinction between naming and the named, and most of our companions hold that the naming is identical to the indicative words and that the name is identical to the meaning. Then these differed: Ibn Furak and others held that every name is exactly the named; thus your saying 'Allah' is a statement indicating a name that is the named, and likewise your saying 'alim' and 'khaliq' because it indicates the Lord described as being knowing and creating. And some said: Among names, some are identical, like 'al-mawjud' and 'al-dhat'; some are other, like 'al-Khaliq,' for the named is His essence and the name is the act of creation itself, and His creation is other than His essence; and some are neither identical nor other, like 'al-'Alim,' for the named is His essence and the name is His knowledge, which is neither identical to His essence nor other than it. The Mu'tazila held that the name is the naming, and some later ones among our companions agreed with them. And the master Abu Nasr ibn Ayyub held that the word 'ism' is equivocal between naming and the named, so it is applied to each and the intended meaning is understood according to context. It is not hidden from you that the dispute according to Abu Nasr's view is only about the word 'ism' and whether it is applied to utterances—in which case naming is in the mentioned sense, not in the sense of the act of the assigner—or to their meanings.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the well-known dispute about the relationship between the name (ism) and the named (musamma). It clarifies that the dispute is not about the linguistic word itself but about its meaning (madlool). Al-Ash'ari is then cited, providing a threefold classification: some names are identical to the named (like 'Allah'), some are other (like 'al-Khaliq' indicating a relation), and some are neither identical nor other (like 'al-'Alim' indicating a real attribute). This classification is based on whether the name indicates the essence alone, a relation, or a real attribute. The passage then moves to al-Amidi, who reports that most Ash'aris hold that tasmiya is the indicative words and ism is the meaning. Among them, Ibn Furak says every name is the named, another group distinguishes types (identical, other, neither), the Mu'tazila identify ism with tasmiya, and Abu Nasr says the word is equivocal. The passage ends by noting that according to Abu Nasr, the dispute is merely verbal about the word 'ism'.

الاسم
المسمى
التسمية
المدلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Furak, what is the relationship between the name 'al-Khaliq' and the named?
A) The name is other than the named.
B) The name is identical to the named.
C) The name is neither identical nor other.
D) The name is the act of creation.

Key Takeaway
Among Ash'aris, Ibn Furak asserts all names are the named, while others adopt a threefold classification similar to al-Ash'ari.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by His names, do you feel you are directly encountering Him, or are the words just mental labels? If you truly believed every name is exactly the named, how would your du'a change?
?
The second group says 'al-'Alim' refers to God's knowledge, which is neither identical to His essence nor other. Does this idea make God's knowledge feel more intimate or more abstract to you? How does it affect your trust in His omniscience?
?
Imagine that a friend uses the name 'al-Khaliq' carelessly, forgetting that it points to the act of creation which is distinct from God. What would you say to them after studying this passage?

Mu'tazila and Abu Nasr on Ism and Tasmiya
Mu'tazila and Abu Nasr on the relationship of ism and tasmiya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili view identifying name with naming and Abu Nasr's view of equivocality.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila equate ism with tasmiya; Abu Nasr says the word is ambiguous.

The debate continues with the Mu'tazila who assert that the name is the naming, meaning the utterance. Some later Ash'aris agreed with them. Abu Nasr offers a linguistic solution: the word 'ism' is equivocal, referring sometimes to the naming and sometimes to the named, with context determining meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the final part of the passage
Read from 'وذهبت المعتزلة' to the end along with its translation. Pay attention to the Mu'tazili view, the agreement of some later Ash'aris, and then Abu Nasr's unique position.
2
Understand the Mu'tazili view
The Mu'tazila hold that the name (ism) is exactly the naming (tasmiya), meaning the utterance itself. This reduces the usual distinction between word and meaning.
Some later Ash'aris followed this view, showing it was not exclusive to the Mu'tazila.
3
Analyze Abu Nasr's solution
Abu Nasr argues that the word 'ism' is equivocal (mushtarak), applied both to the naming (utterance) and to the named (meaning). Context determines which is intended. This implies the dispute is merely verbal about the usage of the word 'ism'.
Abu Nasr's view defuses the theological tension by reframing it as a linguistic issue.

What Is Kalam?

4 min
The Foundational Distinction: Name, Naming, and Named
Introduces the three key terms and the majority Ash'ari definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basic distinction between tasmiya (act of naming) and musammā (the named), and the majority Ash'ari position that tasmiya is the utterance while ism is the indicated meaning.

Core Idea
Naming is the word; the name is the meaning.

Before diving into the intricate debates, we need to establish the basic terms. Al-Amidi reports that all rational people agree on the distinction between the act of naming and the thing named. The Ash'ari majority further clarifies that the act of naming consists of the actual utterances, while the name is the meaning those utterances indicate. Let's examine this foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the terms 'tasmīya', 'ism', and 'musammā'.
2
Identify the three key terms
Find where 'tasmīya' and 'musammā' are defined. Note how al-Amidi's consensus is presented.
3
Understand the Ash'ari majority view
The text says that most Ash'aris consider tasmiya as the utterances that indicate, and ism as the indicated meaning. Consider what this implies about the relationship.
4
Reflect on the implications
Why is this distinction important? Think about how it sets the stage for the later disagreements.

المرصد السابع - المقصد الأول : الاسم غير التسمية
القائمة بذاته ( لا هو ولا غيره كما مر فكذا الحال في الذات المأخوذة مع تلك الصفة قال الآمدي : اتفق العقلاء على المغايرة بين التسمية والمسمى وذهب أكثر أصحابنا إلى أن التسمية هي نفس الأقوال الدالة وأن الاسم هو نفس المدلول، ثم اختلف هؤلاء فذهب ابن فورك وغيره إلى أن كل اسم هو المسمى بعينه فقولك : الله قول دال على اسم هو المسمى وكذا قولك : عالم وخالق فإنه يدل على الرب الموصوف بكونه عالماً وخالفا . وقال بعضهم: من الأسماء ما هو عين كالموجود ،والذات، ومنها ما هو غير كالخالق، فإن المسمى ذاته والاسم هو نفس الخلق وخلقه غير ذاته، ومنها ما ليس عيناً ولا غيراً كالعالم فإن المسمى ذاته والاسم علمه الذي ليس عين ذاته ولا غيرها. وذهبت المعتزلة إلى أن الاسم هو التسمية، ووافقهم على ذلك بعض المتأخرين من أصحابنا، وذهب الأستاذ أبو نصر بن أيوب إلى أن لفظ الاسم مشترك بين التسمية والمسمى فيطلق على كل منهما ويفهم المقصود بحسب القرائن، ولا يخفى عليك أن النزاع على قول أبي نصر إنما هو في لفظة اسم وأنها تطلق على الألفاظ فيكون التسمية بالمعنى المذكور لا بمعنى فعل الواضع أو تطلق على مدلولاتها

قوله : ( فكذا الحال في الذات المأخوذة مع تلك الصفة الظاهر أن المراد بالذات ذات الواجب وفيه أن مفهوم العلم ذات ما له العلم لا ذات الواجب تعالى اللهم إلا أن يراد الذات المطلقة أو يقال : فيه غلبة .
مطلقاً
هو
قوله : ( فذهب ابن فورك إلخ) توضيح المقام أن ابن فورك ومن وافقه أطلقوا الاسم على المدلول المطابقي للفظ، فإن أرادوا بالمسمى ما وضع الاسم بإزائه فوجه إطلاق القول بأن الاسم نفس المسمى ظاهر، وإن أرادوا به ما يطلق عليه الاسم أعني الذات كما يدل عليه قول الشارح فإنه يدل على الذات إلخ . فمرادهم بكون الاسم عين المسمى الاتحاد المعتبر في الحمل والبعض، والذين خالفوه أرادوا بالمسمى المعنى الثاني وأخذوا المدلول أعم من المطابقي . واعتبروا في أسماء الصفات المعاني المقصودة فزعموا أن مدلول الخالق الخلق وأنه غير ذات الخالق بناء على ما في الأمور العامة من أن صفات الأفعال غير الموصوف، وأن الصفات التي لا عينه ولا غيره هي التي يمتنع انفكاكها عن موصوفها ، وأما الشيخ الأشعري فقد الذات أراد بالاسم مدلوله المطابقي وبالمسمى الذات فالمدلول المطابقي للاسم العلمي عين المسمى، وفي نحو الخالق والرازق غيره وفي العالم والقادر لا عينه ولا غيره، فلا حاجة إلى ما ذكره شارح المقاصد لتوجيه كلامه، وهو الذي وجهنا به كلام البعض المخالف لابن فورك ضرورة تصريحهم، بأن الاسم نفس الخلق مثلاً إلا أن يثبت تصريح الشيخ أيضاً به . قوله : ( كالموجود) مبني على أن الوجود عين الذات .
التي
هي
قوله: (هو التسمية وأنه غير المسمى لا يخفي أن جعل الاسم عين التسمية ظاهر البطلان إلا أن يصطلح على ذلك فيكون النزاع معهم
قوله : ( بالمعنى المذكور) أي القول الدال .
لفظياً.
الله فيكون عين المسمى وكلا الاستعمالين ثابت كما في قولك الأسماء والأفعال والحروف، وقوله تعالى : سبح اسم ربك ﴾ [الأعلى : ١] وتبارك اسم ربك [الرحمن : ۷۸]، أي مسماه. وقول لبيد : ثم اسم السلام عليكما. لكن هذا بحث لغوي لا فائدة فيه هاهنا . وقال الإمام الرازي: المشهور عن أصحابنا أن الاسم هو اسم المسمى وعن المعتزلة أنه التسمية وعن ) الغزالي أنه مغاير لهما لأن النسبة وطرفيها متغايرة قطعا والناس قد طولوا في هذه المسألة وهو عندي فضول لأن الاسم هو المخصوص والمسمى ما وضع ذلك اللفظ بإزائه فنقول : الاسم قد يكون غير المسمى، فإن لفظة الجدار مغايرة لحقيقة الجدار وقد يكون عينه، فإن لفظ اسم للفظ الدال على المعنى المجرد عن الزمان، ومن جملة الألفاظ لفظ الاسم فيكون لفظ الاسم اسماً لنفسه، فاتحد هاهنا الاسم أو المسمى، قال : فهذا ما عندي في هذه المسألة
The Seventh Observatory - First Purpose: The name is not the act of naming. The self-subsistent (neither He nor other as previously, so likewise the case for the essence taken with that attribute). Al-Amidi said: The rational people agreed on the distinction between the act of naming (tasmīya) and the named (musammā). Most of our companions held that the act of naming is the very indicative utterances, and that the name (ism) is the very indicated meaning. Then these differed: Ibn Furak and others held that every name is identical to the named itself. Thus your saying 'Allah' is an utterance indicating a name which is the named, and likewise your saying 'Knower' and 'Creator' indicates the Lord described as being knowing and creating. And some said: Among names, some are identical like 'existent' and 'essence', and some are other like 'creator', for the named is His essence and the name is the very creation, and His creation is other than His essence. And among them is that which is neither identical nor other like 'knower', for the named is His essence and the name is His knowledge, which is neither identical to His essence nor other than it. The Mu'tazila held that the name is the act of naming, and they were followed in this by some later scholars among our companions. And the master Abu Nasr ibn Ayyub held that the word 'name' is equivocal between the act of naming and the named, so it is applied to each and the intended meaning is understood by context. It is not hidden from you that the dispute according to Abu Nasr is only about the word 'name' and that it is applied to words, so the act of naming is in the mentioned sense, not in the sense of the act of the positor, or it is applied to their meanings. The name is identical. His saying: (So likewise the case for the essence taken with that attribute). Apparently what is meant by essence is the essence of the Necessary, and in it is that the concept of knowledge is an essence having knowledge, not the essence of the Necessary, exalted is He, unless what is meant is the absolute essence, or it is said: there is a dominance in it. Absolutely it is. His saying: (So Ibn Furak etc.) Clarification of the matter: Ibn Furak and those who agreed with him applied the name to the literal signification of the word. If by the named they meant that for which the name is posited, then the meaning of the statement that the name is the named itself is apparent. If they meant that to which the name is applied, namely the essence, as indicated by the commentator's statement 'it indicates the essence etc.', then they intend by the name being identical to the named the unity considered in predication and partiality. Those who opposed him intended by the named the second meaning and took the signification to be more general than the literal. They considered in the names of attributes the intended meanings, so they claimed that the signification of 'creator' is creation and that it is other than the essence of the Creator, based on what is in general matters: that attributes of actions are other than the described, and that attributes which are neither identical nor other are those whose separation from their subject is impossible. As for al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari, he intended by the name its literal signification and by the named the essence. So the literal signification of the essential name is identical to the named. For something like 'creator' and 'provider', it is other. For 'knower' and 'powerful', it is neither identical nor other. So there is no need for what the commentator of al-Maqasid mentioned to explain his statement. And this is how we explained the statement of some who opposed Ibn Furak, due to their explicit statement that the name is the creation itself, unless al-Shaykh's explicit statement to that effect is also established. His saying: (like 'existent') is based on existence being identical to essence. Which are. His saying: (It is the act of naming and it is other than the named). It is not hidden that making the name identical to the act of naming is clearly false, unless a convention is established, and then the dispute with them would be terminological. His saying: (in the mentioned sense) i.e., the indicative utterance. Verbally. Allah, so it becomes identical to the named, and both usages are established, as in your saying 'nouns, verbs, and particles', and His saying, exalted: 'Glorify the name of your Lord' (Q. 87:1) and 'Blessed be the name of your Lord' (Q. 55:78), i.e., His named. And the saying of Labid: 'Then the name of peace be upon you both.' But this is a linguistic discussion with no benefit here. Imam al-Razi said: The well-known from our companions is that the name is the name of the named, and from the Mu'tazila that it is the act of naming, and from al-Ghazali that it is other than both because the relation and its two terms are necessarily distinct. People have gone on at length about this issue, and in my view it is superfluous, because the name is the particular thing and the named is that for which the word is posited. So we say: The name may be other than the named, for the word 'wall' is other than the reality of the wall, and it may be identical to it, for the word 'name' is for the word that indicates a meaning abstracted from time, and among words is the word 'name', so the word 'name' becomes a name for itself, and here the name and the named are unified. He said: This is what I have on this issue.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive survey of the different scholarly positions on the relationship between the 'name' (ism), the 'named' (musammā), and the 'act of naming' (tasmīya). The author begins by citing al-Amidi that rational consensus affirms a distinction between tasmiya (the act of naming) and musammā (the named thing). He then presents the main view of the Ash'ari school that tasmiya is the actual utterances that indicate, while ism is the meaning indicated. Within the school, Ibn Furak holds that every name is identical to its named: for example, saying 'Allah' indicates a name that is the named itself. Others distinguish: some names are identical (like 'existent' and 'essence'), some are other (like 'creator', where the named is the essence and the name is the act of creation), and some are neither identical nor other (like 'knower', where the named is the essence and the name is knowledge, which is neither identical nor other). The Mu'tazila equate ism with tasmiya. Abu Nasr ibn Ayyub argues that 'ism' is equivocal and understood by context. The commentary then elaborates on Ibn Furak's view: he uses 'ism' for the literal signification of the word, and if 'musammā' means the thing for which the word is posited, identity holds; if it means the essence, then identity is understood as predicative union. Those who oppose him take 'musammā' as the essence and consider signification broader than literal. For attribute names like 'creator', they say the signification is the act of creation, which is other than the essence, following the principle that attributes of action are other than the subject. Al-Ash'ari himself held that the literal signification of an essential name is identical to the named, while for active attributes it is other, and for attributes like 'knower' it is neither. The author dismisses the need for extra explanation from the commentator of al-Maqasid. The discussion of the Quranic verses shows that two usages exist: one where 'name' means the named (as in 'Glorify the name of your Lord'), and one where it refers to the word itself. Al-Razi concludes that the dispute is largely verbal: 'ism' is the particular word, 'musammā' is the thing for which it is posited, and identity or difference depends on the case, with the word 'name' being a name for itself when considered as a word.

مسمى

Checkpoint
According to al-Amidi as cited in the passage, what did the rational people agree upon?
A) The name is identical to the named
B) The act of naming is distinct from the named
C) The name is the same as the act of naming
D) There is no distinction between name and named

Key Takeaway
The act of naming (tasmīya) is the utterance, while the name (ism) is the meaning indicated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the name of Allah in prayer or dhikr, do you ever mistake the sound of the word for the reality of whom you are addressing? How can the Ash'ari distinction between tasmiya (utterance) and ism (meaning) change the way you approach your worship starting tomorrow?
?
Think of a relationship in your life where you have confused the words someone says (their tasmiya) with who they truly are (their ism). What concrete step can you take this week to see beyond labels and engage with the actual person?
?
If the essence of God (musammā) is not confined to the letters of His name, does that humble you or free you? In what specific situation today can you shift your focus from repeating formulas to connecting with the meaning behind them?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
5 min
Internal Ash'ari Disagreements: Ibn Furak and the Threefold Classification
Presents Ibn Furak's identity view and the threefold classification of names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Furak's identity view and the subsequent threefold classification of names as identical, other, or neither in relation to the named.

Core Idea
Some names are identical to the named, some are other, and some are neither.

After establishing the basic definitions, the text presents the first major disagreement within the Ash'ari school. Ibn Furak held that every name is exactly the same as its named. Others refined this view, arguing that names fall into three categories: those identical to the named, those other than it, and those neither identical nor other. Let's explore this nuanced classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Ibn Furak
Focus on the statement that Ibn Furak and others held that every name is identical to the named. Look at the examples given: Allah, Knower, Creator.
2
Understand the threefold classification
Find the categories: identical (like 'existent'), other (like 'creator'), and neither (like 'knower').
3
Analyze the reasoning
For each category, consider why the author puts it in that category. For 'creator', the name is creation, which is other than the essence.
4
Connect to earlier definitions
How does this classification relate to the general rule that ism is the indicated meaning? It shows that the indicated meaning can vary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد السابع - المقصد الأول : الاسم غير التسمية
القائمة بذاته ( لا هو ولا غيره كما مر فكذا الحال في الذات المأخوذة مع تلك الصفة قال الآمدي : اتفق العقلاء على المغايرة بين التسمية والمسمى وذهب أكثر أصحابنا إلى أن التسمية هي نفس الأقوال الدالة وأن الاسم هو نفس المدلول، ثم اختلف هؤلاء فذهب ابن فورك وغيره إلى أن كل اسم هو المسمى بعينه فقولك : الله قول دال على اسم هو المسمى وكذا قولك : عالم وخالق فإنه يدل على الرب الموصوف بكونه عالماً وخالفا . وقال بعضهم: من الأسماء ما هو عين كالموجود ،والذات، ومنها ما هو غير كالخالق، فإن المسمى ذاته والاسم هو نفس الخلق وخلقه غير ذاته، ومنها ما ليس عيناً ولا غيراً كالعالم فإن المسمى ذاته والاسم علمه الذي ليس عين ذاته ولا غيرها. وذهبت المعتزلة إلى أن الاسم هو التسمية، ووافقهم على ذلك بعض المتأخرين من أصحابنا، وذهب الأستاذ أبو نصر بن أيوب إلى أن لفظ الاسم مشترك بين التسمية والمسمى فيطلق على كل منهما ويفهم المقصود بحسب القرائن، ولا يخفى عليك أن النزاع على قول أبي نصر إنما هو في لفظة اسم وأنها تطلق على الألفاظ فيكون التسمية بالمعنى المذكور لا بمعنى فعل الواضع أو تطلق على مدلولاتها

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive survey of the different scholarly positions on the relationship between the 'name' (ism), the 'named' (musammā), and the 'act of naming' (tasmīya). The author begins by citing al-Amidi that rational consensus affirms a distinction between tasmiya (the act of naming) and musammā (the named thing). He then presents the main view of the Ash'ari school that tasmiya is the actual utterances that indicate, while ism is the meaning indicated. Within the school, Ibn Furak holds that every name is identical to its named: for example, saying 'Allah' indicates a name that is the named itself. Others distinguish: some names are identical (like 'existent' and 'essence'), some are other (like 'creator', where the named is the essence and the name is the act of creation), and some are neither identical nor other (like 'knower', where the named is the essence and the name is knowledge, which is neither identical nor other). The Mu'tazila equate ism with tasmiya. Abu Nasr ibn Ayyub argues that 'ism' is equivocal and understood by context. The commentary then elaborates on Ibn Furak's view: he uses 'ism' for the literal signification of the word, and if 'musammā' means the thing for which the word is posited, identity holds; if it means the essence, then identity is understood as predicative union. Those who oppose him take 'musammā' as the essence and consider signification broader than literal. For attribute names like 'creator', they say the signification is the act of creation, which is other than the essence, following the principle that attributes of action are other than the subject. Al-Ash'ari himself held that the literal signification of an essential name is identical to the named, while for active attributes it is other, and for attributes like 'knower' it is neither. The author dismisses the need for extra explanation from the commentator of al-Maqasid. The discussion of the Quranic verses shows that two usages exist: one where 'name' means the named (as in 'Glorify the name of your Lord'), and one where it refers to the word itself. Al-Razi concludes that the dispute is largely verbal: 'ism' is the particular word, 'musammā' is the thing for which it is posited, and identity or difference depends on the case, with the word 'name' being a name for itself when considered as a word.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think all names function the same way in relation to God.
Correction: The passage shows that different types of divine names have different logical relationships to the divine essence.

Checkpoint
According to the threefold classification, which of the following is an example of a name that is 'other' than the named?
A) Existent
B) Knower
C) Creator
D) Essence

Key Takeaway
Names are classified based on their relation to the named: identical, other, or neither.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Knower', do you ever pause to consider what that name really means about your relationship with Him? Does knowing that His knowledge is neither identical to His essence nor separate from it change how you trust Him with your secrets?
?
If the name 'Creator' refers to an act that is distinct from His essence, what does that tell you about the distance between the Creator and His creation? Does that distance humble you or make you feel more awe?
?
Reflect on a time you used a name of Allah like 'The Provider' or 'The Merciful'. Did you treat that name as if it were just a label, or did it actually shape your expectation of His action? How will you let this lesson change the way you call upon Him starting today?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
5 min
The Mu'tazila, Abu Nasr, and al-Razi on the Name-Naming Issue
Presents the Mu'tazila, Abu Nasr, and al-Razi's positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila equating name and naming, Abu Nasr's solution of equivocation, and al-Razi's conclusion that the dispute is largely linguistic.

Core Idea
The debate reduces to whether 'name' refers to the word or the meaning.

The discussion concludes with three more positions: the Mu'tazila claim that name and naming are identical; Abu Nasr argues that the word 'name' is ambiguous; and Imam al-Razi deems the whole dispute superfluous, offering a linguistic resolution. Let's examine these final contributions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila position
Note that they held that the name is the act of naming, and some later Ash'aris agreed.
2
Study Abu Nasr's equivocation view
He claimed the word 'name' is shared between the act of naming and the named, understood by context.
3
Examine al-Razi's analysis
He lists the known positions and dismisses them, proposing his own definition: 'name' is the particular word, 'named' is the thing for which it is posited, and sometimes they coincide.
4
Reflect on the linguistic examples
Consider the Quranic verses and the statement of Labid as evidence for both usages.

المرصد السابع - المقصد الأول : الاسم غير التسمية
القائمة بذاته ( لا هو ولا غيره كما مر فكذا الحال في الذات المأخوذة مع تلك الصفة قال الآمدي : اتفق العقلاء على المغايرة بين التسمية والمسمى وذهب أكثر أصحابنا إلى أن التسمية هي نفس الأقوال الدالة وأن الاسم هو نفس المدلول، ثم اختلف هؤلاء فذهب ابن فورك وغيره إلى أن كل اسم هو المسمى بعينه فقولك : الله قول دال على اسم هو المسمى وكذا قولك : عالم وخالق فإنه يدل على الرب الموصوف بكونه عالماً وخالفا . وقال بعضهم: من الأسماء ما هو عين كالموجود ،والذات، ومنها ما هو غير كالخالق، فإن المسمى ذاته والاسم هو نفس الخلق وخلقه غير ذاته، ومنها ما ليس عيناً ولا غيراً كالعالم فإن المسمى ذاته والاسم علمه الذي ليس عين ذاته ولا غيرها. وذهبت المعتزلة إلى أن الاسم هو التسمية، ووافقهم على ذلك بعض المتأخرين من أصحابنا، وذهب الأستاذ أبو نصر بن أيوب إلى أن لفظ الاسم مشترك بين التسمية والمسمى فيطلق على كل منهما ويفهم المقصود بحسب القرائن، ولا يخفى عليك أن النزاع على قول أبي نصر إنما هو في لفظة اسم وأنها تطلق على الألفاظ فيكون التسمية بالمعنى المذكور لا بمعنى فعل الواضع أو تطلق على مدلولاتها
