The Miracle Must Not Contradict the Claim
Explains the sixth condition with two contrasting examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth condition for miracles and why a miracle that itself testifies against the claimant cannot prove his truthfulness.

Core Idea
A miracle that contradicts the claim invalidates the proof of prophethood.

Sanusi now turns to the sixth condition: the miracle must not itself testify against the claimant. He illustrates this with two cases: a lizard that calls the claimant a liar, and a revived person who denies him. The distinction between the miracle itself and the free action of a person is crucial. This lesson unpacks these examples and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the condition and the lizard example
Start by reading the statement of the sixth condition, then the first example where a lizard speaks and calls the claimant a liar. Notice that the miracle itself becomes the testifier against him.
The lizard has no choice; its speech directly contradicts the claim.
2
Examine the revival case
Now read the second example: reviving a dead person who then denies the claimant. The text discusses why this does not invalidate the miracle, since the revival itself does not deny—only the person does, and he is a free agent.
The key is that the miracle is the act of reviving, not the speech of the revived.
3
Analyze the subcases: life vs. immediate death
The passage considers whether it matters if the revived person lives on and persists in denial, or dies immediately. It presents al-Qadi's view and then the correct view that there is no difference because choice exists in both.
This reinforces that the person's free choice is independent of the miracle.
4
Differentiate from the lizard case
Finally, contrast the revival case with the lizard: in the lizard, the extraordinary event itself is the denial, so it contradicts the claim. In revival, the miracle is the reviving, which does not deny.
The lizard has no choice; the revived person does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : أن لا يكون ما دعاه وأظهره ) من المعجزة ( مكذباً له فلو قال : معجزتي أن ينطق هذا الضب، فقال: إنه كاذب، لم يعلم به صدقه بل ازداد اعتقاد كذبه لأن المكذب هو نفس الخارق ( نعم لو قال : معجزتي أن أحيي هذا الميت فأحياه فكذبه ففيه احتمال والصحيح أنه لا يخرج بذلك عن كونه معجزاً لأن المعجز إحياؤه ) وهو غير مكذب له إنما المكذب هو ذلك الشخص بكلامه ( وهو بعد ذلك ) الإحياء ) مختار في تصديقه وتكذيبه ولم يتعلق به دعوى ) فلا يقدح تكذيبه في دلالة الإحياء على صدقه ( وقيل : (هذا) الذي ذكرنا من عدم خروجه عن كونه معجزاً إنما هو (إذا عاش بعده ) أي بعد الإحياء (زماناً) واستمر على التكذيب . قال الآمدي : لا أعرف في هذه الصورة خلافاً بين الأصحاب ( ولو خر ميتا في الحال ) قال القاضي : ( بطل الإعجاز لأنه كأنه أحيي للتكذيب) فصار مثل تكذيب الضب . ( والحق أنه لا فرق بين استمرار الحياة مع التكذيب وبين عدمه ( لوجود الاختيار في (الصورتين بخلاف الضب ) والظاهر أنه لا يجب تعيين المعجز) بل يكفي أن يقول : أنا آتي بخارق من الخوارق ولا يقدر أحد على أن يأتي بواحد منها. وفي كلام الآمدي أن هذا متفق عليه قال فإذا كان المعجز معيناً فلا بد في معارضته المماثلة وإذا لم يكن معيناً فأكثر الأصحاب على أنه لا بد فيها من المماثلة وقال القاضي : لا حاجة إليها وهو الحق لظهور المخالفة فيما ادعاه .
Sixth: that what he claimed and manifested of the miracle does not testify against him. For if he says: 'My miracle is that this lizard speaks,' and it says: 'He is a liar,' his truthfulness is not known from it; rather, his lying is increased. Because the testifier is the extraordinary event itself. Yes, if he says: 'My miracle is that I revive this dead person,' and he revives him and he (the revived) denies him, then there is a possibility, and the correct view is that it does not remove it from being a miracle, because the miracle is his revivification, and it does not testify against him; rather, the testifier is that person by his speech, and he after revivification is free in affirming or denying him, and no claim is attached to him. So his denial does not impair the indication of revivification for his truthfulness. And it is said: this that we mentioned of its not ceasing to be a miracle is only if he lives after it, i.e., after revivification, for a time and persists in denial. Al-Amidi said: I do not know any disagreement among the companions in this case. And if he dies immediately, al-Qadi said: the miraculousness is invalidated because it is as if he was revived for denial, so it becomes like the denial of the lizard. And the truth is that there is no difference between the continuation of life with denial and its absence, because choice exists in both situations, unlike the lizard. And it is apparent that specifying the miracle is not required; rather, it is sufficient that he says: 'I bring an extraordinary thing from among extraordinary things, and no one is able to bring any one of them.' And in the speech of al-Amidi, this is agreed upon. He said: if the miracle is specified, then opposition must be by similitude; and if it is not specified, most companions hold that similitude is necessary, but al-Qadi said there is no need for it, and that is the truth because the discrepancy in what he claimed is apparent.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a detailed treatment of the sixth condition. It first states that the miracle itself must not testify against the claimant. Two examples illustrate this: (1) If the claimant says a lizard will speak and the lizard calls him a liar, that undermines his claim because the miracle itself becomes a testimony against him. (2) A more complex case: if the claimant revives a dead person, and the revived person then denies the claimant, the miracle is not invalidated because the revival itself does not deny; the denial comes from a free agent. There is a nuance: some say this holds only if the revived person lives on and persists in denial; if he dies immediately, some (like al-Qadi) say the miracle is void. However, the text declares the correct view that there is no difference between living and dying, because choice exists in both, unlike the lizard which has no choice. The passage then adds a related point: specifying the exact miracle is not required; it is enough for the claimant to assert he will bring an extraordinary act beyond anyone's power. There follows a discussion on whether opposition requires similitude: if the miracle is specified, similitude is necessary; if not, most say similitude is needed, but al-Qadi says it is not, and that is deemed correct because the discrepancy is clear.

مكذب
خارق
إحياء
تكذيب
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person who revives a dead person and the revived denies him invalidates the miracle.
Correction: The miracle is the reviving, not the speech; the denial comes from a free agent and does not affect the miracle's validity.

Checkpoint
Why does the lizard's speech in the first example invalidate the miracle, while the revived person's denial in the second does not?
A) Because the lizard is an animal and cannot lie.
B) Because the lizard's speech is the miracle itself, whereas the revival is the miracle and the denial is a separate act.
C) Because the claimant asked the lizard to speak truthfully.
D) Because the lizard's speech is in Arabic.

Key Takeaway
A miracle is not invalidated by the subsequent free speech of a person, only by its own content.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a clear sign of Allah, like the beauty of creation or a miraculous event, do you immediately accept it, or do you let your own subjective feelings and choices cloud your judgment? This lesson shows that a miracle remains valid even if someone denies it; will you commit to recognizing Allah's signs even when others reject them?
?
Think of a time when you witnessed something that clearly pointed to truth but you hesitated because of what others said. In light of this lesson, are you willing to separate the sign itself from the words of people? How can you train yourself to focus on the message of Allah rather than the reactions of creation?
?
If a person denies the truth after witnessing a miracle, does that make the miracle less of a miracle? According to the correct view, no. Does this challenge your assumption that truth must always be accepted? Will you accept that you are responsible for your own choice, and that no amount of external signs forces belief? Make a commitment today to respond to clear truth, regardless of how others around you respond.

Specifying the Miracle and the Need for Similitude
Discusses whether miracles must be specified and the requirement of similitude in opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a prophet need not specify his miracle in advance, and the debate on whether opposition to an unspecified miracle requires similitude.

Core Idea
A prophet may claim a general miracle; if specified, similitude is needed, but al-Qadi says it is not.

Sanusi adds a further point: the claimant does not have to specify exactly what miracle he will perform; it is enough to say he will bring an extraordinary act beyond anyone's power. Then he addresses a related debate: when opponents challenge a miracle, must they produce something similar? The answer depends on whether the miracle was specified, with al-Qadi's view given preference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on specifying the miracle
Start by reading the claim that it is not necessary to specify the exact miracle. The claimant can simply say he will bring an extraordinary thing.
This expands the flexibility of the miracle claim.
2
Understand the agreement on this point
The text says al-Amidi reports consensus on this. Know that the specification is not required.
3
Study the debate on similitude for opposition
Now read the discussion: if the miracle is specified, opposition must match it; if unspecified, most scholars require similitude, but al-Qadi says no need.
The text declares al-Qadi's view as correct because the discrepancy is clear.
4
Reflect on the reasoning
Why does al-Qadi say no similitude is needed? Because when the miracle is unspecified, any impossibility demonstrates the claimant's truth, so it is apparent.
This emphasizes that the miracle's purpose is to prove truth, not to be matched.

The Seventh Condition: Timing of the Miracle
States the seventh condition on the temporal relationship between miracle and claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh condition for miracles: the proper timing of the miracle relative to the claim of prophethood.

Core Idea
A miracle must be timely linked to the claim—neither early nor mismatched.

Theologians set conditions to ensure the miracle directly supports the prophet's claim. The seventh condition focuses on timing: the miracle must not precede the claim, but may accompany it without discrepancy or come after it with specific detail. This lesson examines that condition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice that the text is incomplete—it likely continues with a detail about the delayed miracle.
The passage is a fragment from a longer discussion.
2
Identify the three timing possibilities
The passage lists three temporal relationships: preceding, concomitant, and delayed. See which ones are allowed and which are not.
Preceding is forbidden; concomitant is allowed without discrepancy; delayed is allowed with a detail.
3
Consider why preceding is forbidden
Think about why a miracle before the claim would not serve as a proof. How would it be disconnected from the prophet's message?
This reinforces the link between miracle and prophethood.
4
Reflect on the missing detail
The passage says 'according to a detail' for the delayed miracle. Without the full text, consider what that detail might be. Check your assumptions.
This condition is part of a larger system of conditions for miracles.

Source Text

(السابع : أن لا يكون ) المعجز ) متقدماً على الدعوى مقارناً لها بلا اختلاف أو متأخراً عنها على تفصيل من
(Seventh: that the miracle not be) preceding the claim, or concomitant with it without discrepancy, or delayed after it, according to a detail from
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This is a brief statement of the seventh condition regarding timing. The miracle should not precede the claim of prophethood; it may be simultaneous with the claim without any discrepancy, or it may come after the claim according to a certain detail. The text is cut off, so further elaboration is missing. The condition ensures that the miracle is clearly connected to the claim.

Correction: The miracle must be directly connected to the claim; preceding miracles do not clearly support a future claim.

The Act and the Power
Defends the coherence of the condition on power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a miracle can be within the prophet's power yet still be beyond human capacity, resolving an apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
A miracle's impossibility lies in the power, not the act.

Sanusi defends a subtle condition: the act of a miracle is within the prophet's potential power, but the power to do it is not. This note clarifies that 'within power' does not mean 'possible for others.' Understanding this preserves the integrity of the definition of miracle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice the phrase 'فلا خلل' which signals a defense against a potential objection.
The objection might be that if the act is within the prophet's power, it cannot be a miracle.
2
Identify the analogy
The text compares the act being مقدور (within power) to power itself being غير مقدور (not within power). Understand the parallel: just as the power to create is not created, the act's possibility does not negate the miracle.
Reflect on 'كنفس القدرة في عدم المقدورية'.
3
Apply to the condition
The condition of 'عدم المقدورية' (non-possibility for others) applies to the power, not the act. This note resolves the apparent tension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من للنبى عليه السلام دون غيره معجزة، فلا خلل حينئذ في العبارة ولا يخفى بعد تسليم مساعدة عبارة المصنف، لذلك المعنى أن كون الفعل مقدوراً كنفس القدرة في عدم المقدورية وأنه لا معنى للرد لكونه معجزاً على من شرط عدم مقدورية المعجزة، فتأمل. قوله : (ظاهراً على يد مدعي النبوة) وقولهم كرامة الأولياء معجزة لنبيهم إنما هو بطريق التشبيه أي هي كالمعجزات في دلالتها على صدق نبيهم . قوله : ( وهو بعد ذلك مختاراً إلخ) هذا يدل على أنه لو قال : معجزتي أن أحيي هذا الضب الميت فأحياه فنطق أنه كاذب لا يعلم به صدقه تأمل.
From, for the prophet, peace be upon him, without others, a miracle, so there is no flaw in the phrase, and it is not hidden after accepting the assistance of the author's speech, therefore the meaning is that the act being within his power is like the power itself in non-possibility, and that there is no meaning to the refutation because it is a miracle against whoever stipulated the non-possibility of the miracle, so reflect. His saying: (manifest on the hand of the claimant of prophethood) and their saying that the wonders (karamat) of saints are a miracle for their prophet is only by way of analogy, i.e., they are like miracles in their indication of the truthfulness of their prophet. His saying: (and he after that is free, etc.) this indicates that if he said: 'My miracle is that I revive this dead lizard,' and he revived it and it spoke that he is a liar, his truthfulness is not known from it; reflect.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage consists of several editorial notes. The first note defends the coherence of the earlier discussion: the fact that the act is within the prophet's power does not detract from its miraculousness, because the power itself is not within his power. The analogy is drawn between the act and the power in terms of being beyond human capacity. The second note explains a common phrase: when saints perform wonders (karamat), they are called miracles of the prophet only by analogy, because they indicate the prophet's truthfulness. The third note revisits the sixth condition: if the claimant says his miracle is reviving a dead lizard and it speaks and calls him a liar, then his truthfulness is not known; the note suggests further reflection.

Key Terms
معجزة
مقدور
عدم المقدورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an act is within a prophet's power, it cannot be a miracle because it is possible for him.
Correction: The act is within his power as a human, but the actual power to produce it is from Allah and beyond human capacity. The condition of impossibility applies to others, not to the prophet's own power.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the act being within the prophet's power?
A) It creates a flaw in the definition of miracle.
B) It is like the power itself in being non-possible (for others).
C) It means the miracle is not a true miracle.
D) It only applies to saints, not prophets.

Key Takeaway
The act of a miracle is within the prophet's potential power, but the power to perform it is beyond human capacity, preserving its miraculous nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every good action you perform is enabled by Allah alone, or do you secretly attribute your successes to your own ability? How would your daily gratitude change if you internalized the lesson that even your potential to act is a gift from Him?
?
When you hear about miracles in the Quran or sunnah, do you stop to reflect that the power behind them is entirely from Allah? How can this realization shift your reliance on Him in your own life?
?
The lesson teaches that the act is within the prophet's power, but the power to perform it is not his own. In what areas of your life do you claim a power that is not yours? What would change if you lived in constant awareness that all power belongs to Allah?

Saints' Wonders and a Falsifying Miracle
Clarifies the analogy of saints' miracles and provides a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that saints' wonders are called miracles of the prophet only by analogy, and that a claimed miracle can be falsified by its own content.

Core Idea
Saints' miracles testify to the prophet; some miracles can disprove the claimant.

The text offers two important clarifications. First, the wonders (كرامات) of saints are not independent miracles but are analogically called miracles of the prophet because they confirm his truthfulness. Second, if a claimant says his miracle is reviving a dead lizard and the lizard speaks and calls him a liar, then the event does not prove his truthfulness. These points refine the theology of miracles and prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss on karamat
Focus on the phrase 'بطريق التشبيه' – the relationship is analogical. Understand why saints' wonders point to the prophet.
The saint's miracle is not a proof of his own prophethood but of the prophet's truth.
2
Analyze the lizard example
Consider the hypothetical: a claimant announces a miracle, but the miracle itself declares him a liar. What does this imply?
The miracle must be consistent with the claim; otherwise, it is not a valid miracle.
3
Reflect on the implications
Both points show that the miracle is a sign that must align with the claimant's truthfulness, not an arbitrary event.
The author ends with 'تأمل' – reflection is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من للنبى عليه السلام دون غيره معجزة، فلا خلل حينئذ في العبارة ولا يخفى بعد تسليم مساعدة عبارة المصنف، لذلك المعنى أن كون الفعل مقدوراً كنفس القدرة في عدم المقدورية وأنه لا معنى للرد لكونه معجزاً على من شرط عدم مقدورية المعجزة، فتأمل. قوله : (ظاهراً على يد مدعي النبوة) وقولهم كرامة الأولياء معجزة لنبيهم إنما هو بطريق التشبيه أي هي كالمعجزات في دلالتها على صدق نبيهم . قوله : ( وهو بعد ذلك مختاراً إلخ) هذا يدل على أنه لو قال : معجزتي أن أحيي هذا الضب الميت فأحياه فنطق أنه كاذب لا يعلم به صدقه تأمل.
From, for the prophet, peace be upon him, without others, a miracle, so there is no flaw in the phrase, and it is not hidden after accepting the assistance of the author's speech, therefore the meaning is that the act being within his power is like the power itself in non-possibility, and that there is no meaning to the refutation because it is a miracle against whoever stipulated the non-possibility of the miracle, so reflect. His saying: (manifest on the hand of the claimant of prophethood) and their saying that the wonders (karamat) of saints are a miracle for their prophet is only by way of analogy, i.e., they are like miracles in their indication of the truthfulness of their prophet. His saying: (and he after that is free, etc.) this indicates that if he said: 'My miracle is that I revive this dead lizard,' and he revived it and it spoke that he is a liar, his truthfulness is not known from it; reflect.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage consists of several editorial notes. The first note defends the coherence of the earlier discussion: the fact that the act is within the prophet's power does not detract from its miraculousness, because the power itself is not within his power. The analogy is drawn between the act and the power in terms of being beyond human capacity. The second note explains a common phrase: when saints perform wonders (karamat), they are called miracles of the prophet only by analogy, because they indicate the prophet's truthfulness. The third note revisits the sixth condition: if the claimant says his miracle is reviving a dead lizard and it speaks and calls him a liar, then his truthfulness is not known; the note suggests further reflection.

Key Takeaway
Saints' wonders testify to the prophet's truth by analogy, and a miracle must align with the claimant's truthfulness to be valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The lizard spoke and discredited the claimant. Have you ever ignored an obvious contradiction in someone you admired because you wanted to believe they were right? What would it take for you to accept the evidence even if it shatters your assumption?

The Miracle as a Customary Indication of Truthfulness
Jurjani defines miracle as God's custom of creating knowledge of truthfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school defines a miracle as a customary indication of truthfulness, not a rational proof.

Core Idea
Miracles indicate truthfulness by God's custom, not by rational necessity.

This passage establishes the Ash'ari definition of a miracle (mu'jiza). Jurjani explains that a miracle is not a rational proof of prophethood but a customary sign: God customarily creates knowledge of truthfulness upon its occurrence. The passage clarifies this with an example and defends it against the charge of anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the opening definition: 'God's custom of creating knowledge of truthfulness upon its occurrence.'
Notice that the definition emphasizes custom (ʿādah), not rational necessity.
2
Understand why the indication is customary
The passage explains that a miracle could rationally be performed by a liar, but by custom it is known that it does not. This is key to distinguishing the Ash'ari view from rationalist proofs.
The example of the mountain illustrates how custom produces necessary knowledge.
3
Analyze the analogy and the refutation
The king analogy shows how a contextual sign can be as definitive as explicit speech. Then the passage refutes the objection that this is analogizing God to humans, clarifying that the claim is based on customary necessity, not rational analogy.
This step solidifies the unique Ash'ari position on miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثاني : في حقيقة المعجزة ( إجراء الله عادته بخلق العلم بالصدق عقيبه ) أي عقيب ظهور المعجزة ( فإن إظهار المعجز على يد الكاذب وإن كان ممكناً عقلاً فمعلوم انتفاؤه عادةً) فلا تكون دلالته عقلية لتخلف الصدق عنه في الكاذب بل عادية ( كسائر العاديات لأن قال : أنا نبي ثم نتق الجبل وأوقفه على رؤوسهم ، وقال : إن كذبتموني وقع عليكم وإن صدقتموني انصرف عنكم فكلما هموا بتصديقه بعد عنهم وإذا هموا بتكذيبه قرب منهم علم بالضرورة أنه صادق في دعواه، والعادة قاضية بامتناع ذلك من الكاذب) مع كونه ممكناً عنه إمكاناً عقلياً لشمول قدرته تعالى للمسكنات بأسرها ( وقد ضربوا لهذا مثلاً قالوا : إذا ادعى الرجل بمشهد الجم الغفير أني رسول هذ الملك إليكم ثم قال للملك : إن كنت صادقاً فخالف عادتك وقم من الموضع المعتاد لك من السرير واقعد بمكان لا تعتاده ففعل كان ذلك نازلاً منزلة التصديق بصريح مقاله ولم يشك أحد في صدقه بقرينة الحال وليس (هذا الذي ذكرناه ) من باب قياس الغائب على الشاهد ) حتى يتجه عليه أن الشاهد تعلل أفعال بالأغراض لأنه يراعي المصالح ويدرأ المفاسد بخلاف الغائب إذ لا يبالي بالمصلحة والمفسدة فلا يصح القياس، بل ندعي في إفادته العلم الضرورة العادية أي ندعي أن ظهور المعجز يفيد علما بالصدق وأن كونه مفيدا له معلوم لنا بالضرورة العادية ونذكر هذا المثال للتفيهم وزيادة التقرير.
The first observatory - the second aim: on the reality of the miracle. (God's custom of creating knowledge of truthfulness upon its occurrence) i.e. upon the appearance of the miracle. (For manifesting the miracle at the hand of a liar, though rationally possible, is known by custom to be non-existent). So its indication is not rational because truthfulness would be lacking from it in the liar, but rather customary (like all customary matters. For if he says: I am a prophet, then he shakes the mountain and suspends it over their heads, and says: if you deny me it will fall upon you, and if you believe me it will turn away from you; then every time they intend to believe him it moves away, and every time they intend to deny him it approaches; then it is known necessarily that he is truthful in his claim, and custom judges the impossibility of that from a liar) even though it is rationally possible for him because of the comprehensiveness of God's power over all possible things. (And they gave an example for this: they said: if a man in the presence of a large crowd claims 'I am the messenger of this king to you', then says to the king: 'If I am truthful, contravene your custom and rise from your usual place on the throne and sit in a place you are not accustomed to', and he does so, that would take the place of explicit affirmation of his statement, and no one would doubt his truthfulness because of the contextual evidence. And this (which we have mentioned) is not from the chapter of analogizing the absent from the present) so that it would be objected that the present's actions are motivated by purposes because he considers benefits and repels harms, unlike the absent. Rather, we claim concerning its yielding knowledge the customary necessity, i.e. we claim that the appearance of the miracle yields knowledge of truthfulness, and that it is known to us by customary necessity, and we mention this example for understanding and further confirmation.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage defines the miracle (mu'jiza) according to the Ash'ari school. It begins by stating the definition: God's custom of creating knowledge of truthfulness upon the occurrence of the miracle. Then it explains that the indication of the miracle is not rational but customary: rationally, a miracle could be performed at the hand of a liar, but by custom it is known that it is not. The proof is that if a miracle is performed, it produces necessary knowledge of truthfulness, as in the example of the mountain that moves closer or farther based on belief. The passage also gives an analogy from human kings to illustrate how a contextual sign can be as good as explicit statement. Finally, it refutes the potential objection that this is analogizing the absent (God) from the present (human kings), explaining that the claim is not based on rational analogy but on customary necessity.

معجزة
عاديات
علم ضروري
قياس الغائب على الشاهد
إجراء الله العادة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are rational proofs of prophethood and cannot occur at the hand of a liar.
Correction: Miracles are customary indications; rationally they could occur at a liar's hand, but God's custom prevents it.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
A miracle is a customary sign from God that creates knowledge of the prophet's truthfulness, not a rational proof.

Customary Knowledge and the Hidden Qiyas Objection
Jurjani on customary knowledge and a hidden qiyas objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge from miracles is considered customary and how the objection of a hidden qiyas is addressed.

Core Idea
Miracles produce customary knowledge, not logical necessity.

This lesson unpacks a marginal note by Jurjani, who clarifies that knowledge from miracles follows the same customary pattern as all other proofs. He then addresses a subtle objection: perhaps the impossibility of a miracle occurring from a liar is not just customary but rests on a hidden analogy (qiyas) about the ugliness of lying. The commentary explains why this objection does not undermine the customary nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This passage is a marginal comment, so it assumes you know the main text.
2
Identify the claim that knowledge is customary
Notice that Jurjani first states that knowledge from a miracle is customary, just like all other proofs, even rational ones. Ask: Why might someone think it is different?
The phrase 'العلم بالمدلول حصوله عادي في كل الأدلة' is key.
3
Understand the hidden qiyas objection
The objection suggests that the non-occurrence of a miracle from a liar could be known through a hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) based on the ugliness of lying. Reflect on what this objection implies about the nature of the knowledge.
This objection is a philosophical challenge to the idea that miraculous knowledge is purely customary.
4
See how the objection is addressed
Jurjani responds by affirming that the ugliness of lying is undeniable in terms of praise and blame, but that does not turn the knowledge into a rational proof. The customary nature remains intact.
The phrase 'مما لا ينبغي أن ينكر' shows the defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وهي عندنا إجراء الله تعالى عادته إلخ) فيه أن العلم بالمدلول حصوله عادي في كل الأدلة حتى العقليات عندنا فالأولى الاقتصار على قوله : إظهار المعجزة إلخ . قوله : ( فمعلوم انتفاؤه عادة إلخ) قيل فيه بحث لأن معلومية انتفائه عادة لا ينافي أن يكون بقياس خفي جبلي مجبول في الطبائع وهو قبح تصديق الكاذب من الله تعالى وأنه لا يفعل ذلك لحصول الإضلال الصريح، ثم إن كون الكذب صفة نقص وقبيحا. والصدق صفة كمال وحسناً مما لا ينبغي أن ينكر وإن أنكر بمعنى ترتب الثواب والمدح والعقاب والذم فليتأمل. واعلم أنهم جوزوا ظهور الخوارق على يد المتأله ابتلاء دون المتنبي فإن الأدلة القطعية قائمة على كذبه بخلاف مدعي النبوة ويظهر منه أنهم يجوزون ظهوره على يد من يدعي النبوة بعد خاتم الأنبياء عليه السلام فليتأمل.
His saying: '(And according to us it is God's custom, etc.)' This implies that knowledge of the implicatum is customary in all proofs, even rational ones according to us, so it is better to restrict to his saying: 'manifestation of the miracle etc.' His saying: 'So it is known by custom to be non-existent etc.' It was said that there is investigation in this, because its being known to be non-existent by custom does not negate that it could be by a hidden instinctual qiyas ingrained in natures, namely the ugliness of God's verifying a liar, and that He does not do that because it would result in clear misguidance. Then, that lying is a deficiency and ugly, and truthfulness is perfection and good, is not to be denied, even if it is denied in the sense of the resulting reward and praise and punishment and blame. So reflect. And know that they permitted the appearance of miracles (khawariq) at the hand of a devout person (muta'allih) as a trial, but not at the hand of a false prophet (mutanabbi), because definitive proofs stand on his lying, contrary to the claimant of prophethood. And it appears from this that they permit its appearance at the hand of one who claims prophethood after the Seal of the Prophets, peace be upon him. So reflect.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This is a marginal commentary (qāla) on the previous definition. It first notes that knowledge from a miracle is customary like all evidence, even rational, so the definition could be shortened. Then it addresses an objection: that the customary non-existence of a miracle on a liar could be based on a hidden qiyas (analogy) that lies are ugly and God does not do ugly things. The commentator defends that this is not a problem; the fact that lying is ugly is undeniable in the sense of praise and blame. Finally, it notes that miracles (khawariq) can appear at the hand of a pious non-prophet (muta'allih) as a trial, but not at the hand of a false prophet (mutanabbi), because definitive proofs of his lying exist. However, this implies that they permit miracles at the hand of someone who claims prophethood after the final prophet, which is controversial.

Key Terms
قياس خفي
إضلال
عادته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if knowledge from miracles is based on a hidden qiyas, it becomes rational and not customary.
Correction: The passage clarifies that even if a hidden qiyas exists, the knowledge is still customary because the qiyas itself is instinctive and not a rational demonstration.

Checkpoint
What is the hidden qiyas (hidden analogy) that the objection raises?
A) That miracles are impossible for a liar because it would be ugly for God to verify a liar.
B) That all knowledge is rational and nothing is customary.
C) That miracles can occur at the hand of a false prophet.
D) That customary knowledge is the same as logical necessity.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge from miracles is customary, and the objection based on a hidden qiyas does not change that because the qiyas is not a rational proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see signs of truthfulness in someone's life—like answered prayers or evident blessings—do you trust them because of a rational proof or because of the habitual patterns you have observed in God's ways? Which is more convincing to your heart, and why?
?
The objection suggests an instinctive qiyas that lying is ugly and God would not do that. Have you ever assumed something is impossible for God based on your own moral instincts? How does this passage challenge you to re-examine the basis of that assumption?
?
The commentator says the ugliness of lying is known by custom and revelation, not pure reason. Think of a moral certainty you hold dear. Is it rooted in rational deduction or in the custom of what you have learned from revelation and experience? What does your answer reveal about the source of your most deeply held convictions?

Miracles on the Devout and False Prophets
Jurjani on miracles for muta'allih and mutanabbi, with a sensitive implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between miracles appearing on a devout person (muta'allih) versus a false prophet (mutanabbi), and the implication for claimants after the final prophet.

Core Idea
Miracles can test the devout but never support a false prophet.

This lesson covers the next part of Jurjani's marginal note, which addresses a practical question: can miraculous events (khawariq) occur at the hand of someone who is not a prophet? The answer is yes, but only as a trial (ibtilā'), not as a sign of prophethood. However, they cannot occur at the hand of a false prophet (mutanabbi) because definitive proofs exist that he is lying. The commentator then notes that this implies that miracles could theoretically occur for someone claiming prophethood after the final prophet, which is a sensitive point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the distinction between muta'allih and mutanabbi.
Khawariq means extraordinary events that break the norm.
2
Understand the ruling for muta'allih
The passage states that miracles can appear at the hand of a devout person (muta'allih) as a trial (ibtilā'). Ask: What is the purpose of such a trial?
This shows that miracles are not exclusive proofs of prophethood.
3
Understand the ruling for mutanabbi
Miracles cannot appear at the hand of a false prophet (mutanabbi) because definitive proofs establish his lying. Compare with the muta'allih case.
The key phrase is 'فإن الأدلة القطعية قائمة على كذبه'.
4
Grasp the implication about later claimants
The commentator notes that from this ruling, it appears that they permit miracles for someone claiming prophethood after the final prophet. Reflect on why this is controversial.
This is a subtle point that could be seen as contradicting the finality of prophethood.

The First View of I'jaz: Unique Composition
Introduces the first view: i'jaz is unique composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first classical view that attributes the Qur'an's inimitability (i'jaz) to its unique composition (al-nazm al-gharib), which differs from Arab speech in openings, endings, and verse-pauses.

Core Idea
I'jaz is found in the Qur'an's unprecedented linguistic arrangement.

This lesson begins our exploration of the aspects of the Qur'an's miraculous nature. The author introduces the topic by dividing it into two chapters: the nature of i'jaz and the objections against it. We then encounter the first scholarly view, which locates the miracle in the Qur'an's singular composition—a form of speech that broke all precedents of Arab poetry and prose.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author announces a two-chapter structure.
2
Identify the first view
The passage presents the first view: i'jaz is due to 'al-nazm al-gharib' (unique composition). Find this phrase and understand its meaning.
This view is attributed to some Mu'tazila.
3
List the three features
The passage specifies three features of the unique composition: openings (matali'), endings (maqati'), and pauses (fawasil). Identify each in the Arabic and note their definitions.
4
Reflect on the argument
Ask yourself: why do these features make the Qur'an inimitable? The passage says they occurred in a manner not known in their speech, and they were incapable of it.
This connects to the broader theme of i'jaz as a linguistic miracle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنتكلم الآن في وجه إعجازه وفي شبه القادحين فيه في فصلين الفصل الأول في وجه إعجازه وقد اختلف فيه على مذاهب فقيل : هو ما اشتمل عليه من النظم الغريب أي التأليف المخالف لنظم العرب ونثرهم في مطالعها أي أوائل السور والقصص وغيرها ومقاطعها أي أواخرها وفواصلها أي أخر الآي التي هي بزنة الإسجاع في كلامهم فإن هذه الأمور المذكورة وقعت في القرآن على وجه لم يعهد في كلامهم وكانوا عاجزين عنه وعليه بعض المعتزلة.
And let us now speak about the aspect of its inimitability (i'jaz) and about the doubts of the objectors against it in two chapters. The first chapter: on the aspect of its inimitability. There is disagreement over it into schools. It was said: It is what it comprises of the strange composition (al-nazm al-gharib)—i.e., the arrangement—opposing the composition of the Arabs and their prose in its openings, i.e., the beginnings of the surahs and stories and others, and its endings, i.e., its conclusions, and its pauses, i.e., the ends of the verses which are of the meter of saj' in their speech. For these mentioned matters occurred in the Qur'an in a manner not known in their speech, and they were incapable of it. And upon this view is some of the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage introduces the discussion on the miraculous aspect of the Qur'an (wajh i'jaz). The author states that the topic will be treated in two chapters: the first on the aspect of i'jaz itself, and the second on doubts raised against it. He then notes that scholars have differed over the precise nature of this miraculous aspect, listing several schools. The first view, held by some Mu'tazila, is that the i'jaz lies in the Qur'an's unique composition (al-nazm al-gharib). This composition is distinct from both the poetry and prose of the Arabs, differing in its openings, endings, and verse-pauses (fawasil). These features appear in a way never before witnessed in Arabic speech, rendering the Arabs incapable of producing its like.

Key Terms
النظم الغريب
المطالع
المقاطع
الفواصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: I'jaz is merely about the content of the Qur'an, not its linguistic form.
Correction: The first view emphasizes that i'jaz lies specifically in the unique formal composition—the arrangement of openings, endings, and pauses—which is unlike any Arab speech.

Checkpoint
According to the first view mentioned, what is the aspect of the Qur'an's inimitability?
A) Its eloquence (balaghah)
B) Its unique composition (nazm gharib)
C) Its prophecies of the unseen
D) Its lack of contradiction

Key Takeaway
The first view holds that the Qur'an's miraculous nature lies in its unprecedented linguistic composition, contrasting with all Arab speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This view attributes i'jaz to the form, not just the content. In your own spiritual practice, do you pay attention to the linguistic beauty of the Qur'an as an evidence of its divine origin? What step can you take today to study or reflect on the nazm of a single surah?

Second View: I'jaz Through Supreme Eloquence
Presents the second view and defines balaghah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second view on i'jaz and the definition of balaghah as splendid expression about sound meaning.

Core Idea
Balaghah combines flawless words with context-appropriate meaning.

This lesson introduces the second major theory of i'jaz: that the Qur'an's inimitability lies in its supreme eloquence, which the Arabs could not match. We will explore the definition of balaghah given by al-Jahiz and the Arabic scholars, and see how it sets the standard for the Qur'an's uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the second view
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that the second view is attributed to al-Jahiz and the people of Arabic language.
The passage begins with 'وقيل' signaling a different opinion.
2
Understand the definition of balaghah
Focus on the sentence: 'البلاغة التعبير باللفظ الرائع عن المعنى الصحيح'. This is the best definition according to the author.
Splendid word means free from defects; sound meaning means appropriate to context.
3
Connect definition to i'jaz
The Qur'an's eloquence is unparalleled in its nobility of words and precision of meaning, hence inimitable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : وجه إعجازه كونه في الدرجة العالية من البلاغة التي لم يعهد مثلها في تراكيبهم وتقاصرت عنها درجات بلاغتهم وعليه الجاحظ وأهل العربية ثم إنهم قالوا في تفسير البلاغة عبارات مختلفة أحسنها قولهم : البلاغة التعبير باللفظ الرائع أي المعجب بخلوصه عن معايب المفردات وتأليفاتها واشتماله على منافيها عن المعنى الصحيح أي المناسب للمقام الذي أورد فيه الكلام بلا زيادة ولا نقصان في البيان والدلالة عليه وعلى هذا فكلما ازداد شرف الألفاظ ورونق المعاني ومطابقة الدلالة كان الكلام أبلغ وهل رتب البلاغة متناهية اختلفوا فيه والحق أن الموجود منها متناه لأنها واقعة في تلك الألفاظ الشريفة الدالة على المعاني الصحيحة، ولا شك أن الموجود من تلك الألفاظ في اللغات متناه دون الممكن من مراتبها فإنه غير متناه إذ لا يتعذر وجود الفاظ هي أفصح من الألفاظ الواقعة وأشد مطابقة لمعانيها فتكون أعلى رتبة في البلاغة وهكذا إلى ما لا يتناهى.
And it was said: The aspect of its inimitability is its being in the high degree of eloquence (al-balaghah) whose like was not known in their compositions and the degrees of their eloquence fell short of it. Upon this view is al-Jahiz and the people of Arabic. Then they said, in explaining eloquence, different expressions, the best of which is their saying: Eloquence is expression with a splendid word—i.e., admirable for its purity from defects of individual words and their combinations and its inclusion of their opposites—about a sound meaning—i.e., appropriate to the context in which the speech is delivered, without increase or decrease in clarification and indication. Upon this, whenever the nobility of words, the brilliance of meanings, and the correspondence of indication increase, the speech is more eloquent. And whether the ranks of eloquence are finite—they differed over it. The truth is that what exists of it is finite, because it occurs in those noble words that indicate sound meanings, and there is no doubt that what exists of those words in languages is finite, while the possible among its ranks is infinite, since it is not impossible that words exist that are more eloquent than the words that occur and more strongly corresponding to their meanings, thus being higher in rank of eloquence, and so on ad infinitum.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents the second view on the aspect of i'jaz, attributed to al-Jahiz and the Arabic linguists: the Qur'an's inimitability lies in its surpassing eloquence (balaghah), which the Arabs could not match. The author then explains the definition of balaghah, noting various interpretations, the best being 'expression with a splendid word about a sound meaning.' A splendid word is pure from defects and fit for the meaning; sound meaning is appropriate to the context without excess or deficiency. Thus, the more noble the words and the more precise the indication, the greater the eloquence. The author then addresses whether the degrees of eloquence are finite. The truth is that actual existing eloquence is finite, as it is realized in finite words. However, possible degrees of eloquence are infinite, because one can always conceive of more eloquent expressions. This sets the stage for the argument that the Qur'an's eloquence is at the highest actual degree.

Key Terms
البلاغة
اللفظ الرائع
المعنى الصحيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think balaghah is purely subjective and has no objective criteria.
Correction: Balaghah is defined with objective criteria: the word must be splendid (free from defects) and the meaning must be sound (appropriate to context).

Checkpoint
According to the definition given, what two components does balaghah combine?
A) Elegant words and figurative meanings
B) Splendid expression and sound meaning
C) Poetry and prose
D) Brevity and clarity

Key Takeaway
The second view holds that the Qur'an's inimitability is due to its supreme eloquence, defined as expression with a splendid word and sound meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah, are you conscious of using words that are pure and meanings that fit the context? How often do you check your speech for clarity and appropriateness in explaining matters of faith?
?
This passage defines balaghah as expression with a splendid word about a sound meaning. In your daily conversations about religion, do you prioritize this standard? If not, what would need to change?
?
The definition requires no increase or decrease in clarification. Reflect on a time you either overstated or understated in discussing Islam. How can you commit to being more precise?

Are the Ranks of Eloquence Finite or Infinite?
Discusses whether the ranks of eloquence are finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether the ranks of eloquence are finite, and why the actual existing ranks are finite while possible ranks are infinite.

Core Idea
Actual eloquence is finite; possible eloquence is infinite.

Having defined balaghah, the author now addresses a scholarly dispute: are the degrees of eloquence limited? This lesson examines the argument that existing eloquence is finite because it relies on finite words, but that one can always conceive of more eloquent expressions, leading to an infinite potential.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage noting the question of finiteness
Read the Arabic and translation. The passage begins with 'وهل رتب البلاغة متناهية' - whether the ranks of eloquence are finite.
The author mentions a difference of opinion.
2
Understand the distinction between actual and possible
The truth is that what exists (الموجود) of eloquence is finite because it occurs in noble words that are finite in language. However, the possible (الممكن) ranks are infinite because one can always conceive of more eloquent words.
This distinction resolves the debate.
3
Reflect on the implication for i'jaz
The Qur'an represents the highest actual rank of eloquence, which is finite and unattainable by others, while the possibility of higher eloquence in theory does not diminish its inimitability.

The Qur'an's Comprehensive Eloquence and Human Incapacity
Explains that the Qur'an contains all types of balaghah, while human orators master only one or two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Qur'an's encompassing of all rhetorical types demonstrates its miraculous nature, while human orators are limited to one or two types.

Core Idea
The Qur'an contains every type of balaghah perfectly; no human can match that.

This passage from al-Mawaqif explains a key argument for the Qur'an's miraculous nature (i'jaz). It asserts that the Qur'an exhibits all branches of eloquence—concision, metaphor, emphasis, etc.—in their most excellent forms. In contrast, even the greatest Arab poets and orators can excel in only one or two types. This comprehensive coverage of rhetorical techniques is a clear sign of divine origin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice that the passage begins by stating that the eloquence of the Qur'an is agreed upon.
2
Identify the list of balaghah types
The passage enumerates several rhetorical devices found in the Qur'an. Locate each one: conveying many meanings concisely, emphasis, simile, metaphor, etc.
The list includes 'beauty of openings and endings' and 'appropriate conjunction and disjunction'.
3
Compare with human ability
Notice the contrast: the Qur'an has all types, but even the most eloquent human can master only one or two. This contrast is the core of the proof.
Al-Amidi's quotation reinforces this point.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: why does this comprehensive eloquence prove inimitability? The commentary will help you see that it points to divine authorship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أصل البلاغة في القرآن متفق عليه لا ينكره من له أدنى تمييز ومعرفة بصياغة الكلام، وأما كونه في الدرجة العالية غير المعتادة وبهذا القدر يحصل الإعجاز الذي هو مطلوبنا ولا حاجة بنا في إثبات إعجازه إلى بيان أنه الغاية القصوى في المراتب الممكنة من البلاغة فلان وأما كونه في الدرجة العالية الخارجة العادة فثابت لأن من تتبع القرآن من العارفين بالبلاغة وجد فيه فنونها بأسرها من إفادة المعاني الكثيرة باللفظ القليل، ومن ضروب التأكيد وأنواع التشبيه والتمثيل أي ضرب المثل وأصناف الاستعارة وحسن المطالع والمقاطع من الكلام وحسن الفواصل والتقديم والتأخير والفصل والوصل اللائق بالمقام وتعريه أي خلوه عن اللفظ الغث أي الركيك والشاذ الخارج عن القياس والشارد النافر عن الاستعمال إلى غير ذلك من أنواع البلاغات بحيث أي وجده مشتملاً على فنون البلاغة بحيث لا يرى المتصفح له أي للقرآن وتراكيبه المميز بين فنون البلاغة نوعاً منها أي من تلك الفنون إلا وجده فيه أحسن ما يكون فالقرآن مشتمل على جملتها لم يغادر شيئاً منها ولا يقدر أحد من البلغاء الواصلين إلى ذروة البلاغة من العرب العرباء وإن استفرغ وسعه وطاقته في تزيين كلامه إلا على نوع أو نوعين منه أي من المذكور الذي هو فنون البلاغة وربما لو رام غيره أي غير ذلك النوع لم يواته أي لم يوافقه ولم يتأت له. قال الآمدي : إن أفصح فصيح من العرب وأبلغ بليغ من أهل الأدب من أرباب النظم والنثر والخطب غايته الاستئثار بنوع واحد من أنواع البلاغة على وجه لو رام غيره في كلامه لما واتاه وكان فيه مقصراً والقرآن محتو عليها كلها.
Then, the principle of eloquence in the Qur'an is agreed upon, not denied by anyone having the least discernment and knowledge of the formulation of speech. As for its being in the high, unaccustomed degree, and to this extent the inimitability which is our goal is achieved, and we have no need in proving its inimitability to demonstrate that it is the ultimate extreme in the possible ranks of eloquence—because, and as for its being in the high degree beyond custom, it is proven because whoever studies the Qur'an from those knowledgeable in eloquence finds in it all its types: from conveying many meanings with few words, and from kinds of emphasis, types of simile and allegory—i.e., striking of examples—and varieties of metaphor, beauty of openings and endings of speech, beauty of pauses, fronting and delaying, separation and connection appropriate to the context, and its being free from the weak word—i.e., the feeble and anomalous, the outlier from analogy and the refractory, fleeing from usage—and so on from the types of eloquence. Such that the one who peruses it—i.e., the Qur'an and its compositions—discriminating between the types of eloquence, does not find a single type of them except he finds it in it in the best possible way. So the Qur'an encompasses all of them, leaving none out. And none of the eloquent reaching the pinnacle of eloquence from the pure Arabs, even if he exhausts his capacity and energy in embellishing his speech, can [attain] except one or two types of what has been mentioned, which are the types of eloquence. Perhaps if he aims for another type, it does not suit him—i.e., does not agree with him and is not achieved. Al-Amidi said: The most eloquent of the eloquent Arabs and the most fluent of the literary people, masters of poetry, prose, and sermons—his utmost is to excel in one type of eloquence, such that if he aims for another in his speech, it does not suit him and he is deficient therein. And the Qur'an contains all of them.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the second view, emphasizing that the Qur'an's high degree of eloquence—beyond custom—is sufficient to prove its i'jaz, without needing to argue that it is the absolute highest possible. The author explains that those knowledgeable in balaghah find in the Qur'an all its branches: conveying many meanings concisely, various types of emphasis, simile, metaphor, excellent openings and endings, apt fronting and delaying, appropriate conjunction/disjunction, and freedom from weak or anomalous words. In fact, every type of eloquence is found in the Qur'an in its best form. In contrast, even the most eloquent Arab orator can master only one or two types; if he attempts another, he fails. Al-Amidi's statement reinforces this: the pinnacle for any human is to excel in a single type, while the Qur'an contains them all. This comprehensive coverage demonstrates its divine origin.

Key Terms
فنون البلاغة
المعاني الكثيرة باللفظ القليل
الفصل والوصل
اللفظ الغث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eloquence is purely subjective and cannot be objectively measured or compared.
Correction: The passage shows that eloquence consists of identifiable rhetorical techniques (e.g., conciseness, metaphor) that can be enumerated and mastered. The Qur'an's comprehensive inclusion of all such techniques demonstrates an objective superiority.

Checkpoint
According to al-Amidi, what is the utmost that the most eloquent Arab can achieve in eloquence?
A) Mastering three or four types
B) Excelling in one type
C) Equaling the Qur'an in eloquence
D) Repeating the same style in all speech

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's comprehensive mastery of all rhetorical arts, unmatched by any human, is a clear proof of its divine origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Qur'an, do you ever pause to marvel at its unique blend of styles—concise yet profound, firm yet comforting? How does the fact that no human can combine all these rhetorical arts change your approach to the Qur'an? Will you now read it not just for meaning but to witness the miracle of its speech?
?
You probably consider yourself a good speaker or writer in your own language. But this lesson shows that even the greatest Arab orators could master only one or two rhetorical types. How does this humble your pride in your own eloquence? Will you acknowledge that true eloquence belongs only to Allah?
?
The passage says the Qur'an is free from weak or anomalous words. Have you ever noticed how its words feel 'just right'? When you speak or write, do you care about the quality of your words? How can you let the Qur'an's perfection refine your own speech?

The Quran's Comprehensive Eloquence as a Miracle
Describes the Quran's comprehensive eloquence and its role in proving i'jaz.

Core Idea
The Quran contains all arts of eloquence, which no human can match, making it a miracle.

This lesson examines the detailed description of the Quran's eloquence provided by the author. It demonstrates that the Quran contains every recognized art of rhetoric—concision, metaphor, simile, emphasis, and more—in their highest form, while no human orator can achieve more than one or two types. This comprehensive eloquence establishes the Quran's i'jaz.

1

2

المرصد الأول - المقصد الرابع: في إثبات نبوة محمد. في المراتب الممكنة من البلاغة ( فلان ) أي وأما كونه في الدرجة العالية الخارجة العادة فثابت لأن من تتبع القرآن من العارفين بالبلاغة وجد فيه فنونها بأسرها من إفادة المعاني الكثيرة باللفظ القليل، و (من) ضروب التأكيد وأنواع التشبيه والتمثيل ( أي ضرب المثل ) و أصناف الاستعارة وحسن المطالع والمقاطع من الكلام و حسن الفواصل والتقديم والتأخير والفصل والوصل اللائق بالمقام وتعريه أي خلوه عن اللفظ الغث أي الركيك والشاذ الخارج عن القياس والشارد النافر عن الاستعمال إلى غير ذلك من أنواع البلاغات. بحيث أي وجده مشتملاً على فنون البلاغة بحيث لا يرى المتصفح له أي للقرآن وتراكيبه المميز بين فنون البلاغة نوعاً منها أي من تلك الفنون إلا وجده فيه أحسن ما يكون فالقرآن مشتمل على جملتها لم يغادر شيئاً منها ولا يقدر أحد من البلغاء الواصلين إلى ذروة البلاغة من العرب العرباء وإن استفرغ وسعه وطاقته في تزيين كلامه إلا على نوع أو نوعين منه أي من المذكور الذي هو فنون البلاغة وربما لو رام غيره أي غير ذلك النوع لم يواته أي لم يوافقه ولم يتأت له. قال الآمدي: إن أفصح فصيح من العرب وأبلغ بليغ من أهل الأدب من أرباب النظم والنثر والخطب غايته الاستئثار بنوع واحد من أنواع البلاغة على وجه لو رام غيره في كلامه لما واتاه وكان فيه مقصراً والقرآن محتو عليها كلها. ومن كان أعرف بالعربية أي لغة العرب وفنون بلاغتها كان أعرف بإعجاز القرآن المتفرع على بلاغته. وقال القاضي الباقلاني هو: أي وجه إعجازه مجموع الأمرين أي النظم الغريب وكونه في الدرجة العالية من البلاغة. وقيل: هو إخباره عن الغيب نحو (وهم من بعد غلبهم سيغلبون في بضع سنين) [الروم:٣] أخبر عن غلبة الروم على الفرس فيما بين الثلاث إلى التسع وقد وقع كما أخبر به وذلك كثيراً يعرف بتتبع القرآن وإخباراته عن الأمور المستقلة الكائنة على وفقها. وقيل: وجه إعجازه عدم اختلافه وتناقضه مع ما فيه من الطول والامتداد وتمسكوا في ذلك بقوله تعالى: (ولو كان من عند غير الله لوجدوا فيه اختلافاً كثيراً) [النساء:۸۲]. وقيل: إعجازه بالصرفة على أن العرب كانت قادرة على كلام مثل القرآن قبل البعثة لكن الله صرفهم عن معارضته واختلف في كيفية الصرف. فقال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق منا والنظام من المعتزلة: صرفهم الله عنها مع قدرتهم عليها وذلك بأن صرف دواعيهم إليها مع كونهم مجبولين عليها خصوصاً عند توفر الأسباب الداعية في حقهم كالتقريع بالعجز والاستنزال عن الرياسات والتكليف بالانقياد فهذا الصرف خارق للعادة فيكون معجزاً. وقال المرتضى من الشيعة: بل صرفهم بأن سلبهم العلوم التي يحتاج إليها في المعارضة يعني أن المعارضة والإتيان بمثل القرآن يحتاج إلى علوم يقتدر بها عليها، وكانت تلك العلوم حاصلة لهم لكنه تعالى سلبها عنهم فلم يبق لهم قدرة عليها. قوله: (وقيل بالصرفة) رد عليه بأن الأنسب حينئذ ترك الاعتبار ببلاغته لأنه كلما كان أنزل في البلاغة كان عدم تيسر المعارضة أبلغ في الإعجاز.
First Observatory - Fourth Objective: On Establishing the Prophethood of Muhammad. Regarding the possible ranks of eloquence: (such) means that its being in the high, extraordinary degree is established, because whoever examines the Quran from among those knowledgeable in eloquence finds all its arts in it, from conveying many meanings with few words, and from types of emphasis, kinds of simile and representation (i.e., striking a likeness), and categories of metaphor, good openings and closures of speech, good rhymes (fasila), precedence and postponement, conjunction and disjunction appropriate to the context, and its being free from poor (i.e., weak) wording, anomalous (outside analogy), and rare (deviant from usage) — to other types of eloquence. Such that, i.e., he finds it containing the arts of eloquence such that the examiner of it (i.e., of the Quran and its constructions), the distinguisher between the arts of eloquence, does not see a single type of them (i.e., of those arts) except he finds it in the Quran in the best possible way. So the Quran includes all of them, not leaving any of them. And no one of the eloquent speakers who have reached the pinnacle of eloquence among the pure Arabs, even if he exhausts his capacity and ability in embellishing his speech, can (achieve) more than one or two types of it (i.e., of the mentioned arts of eloquence). And perhaps if he sought another (i.e., another type), it would not assist him (i.e., would not cooperate with him or be feasible for him). Al-Amidi said: The most eloquent of the Arabs and the most skilled in literature from the masters of poetry, prose, and sermons—his utmost is to exclusively possess one type of eloquence, such that if he sought another in his speech it would not assist him and he would be deficient therein. But the Quran contains them all. And whoever is more knowledgeable in Arabic (i.e., the language of the Arabs and its arts of eloquence) is more knowledgeable in the miraculous nature (i'jaz) of the Quran, which branches from its eloquence. Al-Qadi al-Baqillani said: It (i.e., the aspect of its i'jaz) is the combination of two matters: the extraordinary style (al-nazm al-gharib) and its being at the highest degree of eloquence. And it was said: It is its informing of the unseen, like (And they will after their defeat overcome in a few years) (al-Rum 3-4); He informed of the Romans' victory over the Persians within three to nine years, and it occurred as He informed. And that is much, known by examining the Quran and its reports about future matters that occurred accordingly. And it was said: The aspect of its i'jaz is its lack of discrepancy and contradiction despite its length and extension; they held to that by His saying: (Had it been from other than Allah, they would have found therein much contradiction) (al-Nisa 82). And it was said: Its i'jaz is by al-sarfa (divine prevention), meaning that the Arabs were capable of speech like the Quran before the mission, but Allah prevented them from opposing it. They differed on the modality of al-sarfa. The master Abu Ishaq from us and al-Nazzam from the Mu'tazila said: Allah prevented them from it despite their ability, by turning their motivations away from it, despite their innate disposition toward it, especially when motives for opposition were present, such as rebuke for inability, removal from leadership, and obligation of submission. So this prevention is miraculous (khariq al-'ada) and thus a miracle. And al-Murtada from the Shi'a said: Rather, He prevented them by stripping them of the sciences needed for opposition, meaning that opposing and producing something like the Quran requires sciences by which one can accomplish it, and those sciences had been available to them, but Allah removed them, so they no longer had ability. The saying: (And it was said by al-sarfa) is refuted by the fact that then it would be more appropriate to disregard its eloquence, because the lower its eloquence, the greater the prevention would be a miracle.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage enumerates the various scholarly opinions on the precise aspect (wajh) of the Quran's miraculous inimitability (i'jaz). It begins by describing the Quran's eloquence in detail: it contains all arts of eloquence—concision, emphasis, simile, metaphor, good structure, rhymes, word order, and freedom from weak or anomalous expressions. The author explains that no human orator can achieve more than one or two of these arts, quoting al-Amidi to that effect. Then he presents five views on wajh al-i'jaz: (1) Al-Qadi al-Baqillani combines the extraordinary style and superlative eloquence. (2) Some say it is the report of the unseen, citing the Romans' victory. (3) Others say it is the lack of contradiction (quoting Quran 4:82). (4) The theory of al-sarfa (divine prevention): God prevented the Arabs from opposing the Quran despite their ability. Two sub-views are given: Abu Ishaq and al-Nazzam say God turned their motivations away, while al-Murtada (Shi'i) says God removed their knowledge. (5) A marginal note refutes al-sarfa: if God simply prevented them, then low eloquence would make the prevention more miraculous, which is inappropriate for the Quran's high eloquence.

Key Terms
إعجاز
بلاغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many types of eloquence can a human orator achieve at most?
A) One or two
B) Three
C) All
D) None

Key Takeaway
The Quran's comprehensive and unmatched eloquence is a fundamental aspect of its miraculous nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Quran claims to contain all arts of eloquence. If you were to examine it with this claim in mind, what would you look for, and how would it change your relationship with the Quran?
?
You have heard that no human can match the Quran's eloquence. Have you ever sincerely tested this challenge? If not, why do you shy away from putting your own speech to the test?
?
The passage says knowledge of Arabic increases knowledge of i'jaz. What effort have you made to learn Arabic to deepen your conviction? Will you commit to taking a step toward that today?

What Is Kalam?
beginner
3 min
Al-Baqillani's Combined View of Miracle
Quotes al-Qadi al-Baqillani's opinion on the aspect of i'jaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Qadi al-Baqillani's view that the Quran's miraculousness is a combination of its extraordinary style and its highest degree of eloquence.

Core Idea
The Quran's miracle combines unique style and supreme eloquence.

This lesson presents the opinion of al-Qadi al-Baqillani, who synthesizes two elements: the unusual arrangement of words (al-nazm al-gharib) and the supreme level of eloquence. This view is a middle ground between focusing solely on style or content.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Baqillani's statement
Read the Arabic sentence and translation where al-Baqillani defines the aspect of i'jaz as the combination of two matters.
2
Identify the two components
Note the two components: the extraordinary style (النظم الغريب) and the high degree of eloquence (الدرجة العالية من البلاغة).
This combines form and content.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this combination is noteworthy. It avoids reducing the miracle to just style or just eloquence.
This is a comprehensive view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

What Is Kalam?
beginner

Reports of the Unseen as Miraculous Aspect
Presents the opinion that Quranic prophecies of the unseen are a miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the Quran's prophecies about future events, such as the Roman victory, constitute a distinct aspect of its miraculous inimitability.

Core Idea
The Quran's accurate reports of the unseen are a miracle.

This lesson covers the view that the Quran's miracle lies in its news about future events that later come true. The author gives the example of the Romans' victory over the Persians, predicted in Surah al-Rum, which occurred exactly as foretold. This is presented as one of several proposed aspects of i'jaz.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on unseen reports
Read the Arabic and translation explaining that the Quran informs about the unseen, with the example of the Romans.
The Quranic verse is referenced: (وهم من بعد غلبهم سيغلبون).
2
Understand the argument
Note that the fulfillment of such prophecies, which no human could have known, demonstrates divine origin.
Many such prophecies exist in the Quran.
3
Connect to i'jaz
Recognize that this is one of several opinions on the aspect of i'jaz, each highlighting a different dimension.
This view focuses on knowledge of the unseen.

والوصل اللائق بالمقام وتعريه أي خلوه عن اللفظ الغث أي الركيك والشاذ الخارج عن القياس والشارد النافر عن الاستعمال إلى غير ذلك من أنواع البلاغات. بحيث أي وجده مشتملاً على فنون البلاغة بحيث لا يرى المتصفح له أي للقرآن وتراكيبه المميز بين فنون البلاغة نوعاً منها أي من تلك الفنون إلا وجده فيه أحسن ما يكون فالقرآن مشتمل على جملتها لم يغادر شيئاً منها ولا يقدر أحد من البلغاء الواصلين إلى ذروة البلاغة من العرب العرباء وإن استفرغ وسعه وطاقته في تزيين كلامه إلا على نوع أو نوعين منه أي من المذكور الذي هو فنون البلاغة وربما لو رام غيره أي غير ذلك النوع لم يواته أي لم يوافقه ولم يتأت له. قال الآمدي: إن أفصح فصيح من العرب وأبلغ بليغ من أهل الأدب من أرباب النظم والنثر والخطب غايته الاستئثار بنوع واحد من أنواع البلاغة على وجه لو رام غيره في كلامه لما واتاه وكان فيه مقصراً والقرآن محتو عليها كلها. ومن كان أعرف بالعربية أي لغة العرب وفنون بلاغتها كان أعرف بإعجاز القرآن المتفرع على بلاغته. وقال القاضي الباقلاني هو: أي وجه إعجازه مجموع الأمرين أي النظم الغريب وكونه في الدرجة العالية من البلاغة. وقيل: هو إخباره عن الغيب نحو (وهم من بعد غلبهم سيغلبون في بضع سنين) [الروم:٣] أخبر عن غلبة الروم على الفرس فيما بين الثلاث إلى التسع وقد وقع كما أخبر به وذلك كثيراً يعرف بتتبع القرآن وإخباراته عن الأمور المستقلة الكائنة على وفقها. وقيل: وجه إعجازه عدم اختلافه وتناقضه مع ما فيه من الطول والامتداد وتمسكوا في ذلك بقوله تعالى: (ولو كان من عند غير الله لوجدوا فيه اختلافاً كثيراً) [النساء:۸۲]. وقيل: إعجازه بالصرفة على أن العرب كانت قادرة على كلام مثل القرآن قبل البعثة لكن الله صرفهم عن معارضته واختلف في كيفية الصرف. فقال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق منا والنظام من المعتزلة: صرفهم الله عنها مع قدرتهم عليها وذلك بأن صرف دواعيهم إليها مع كونهم مجبولين عليها خصوصاً عند توفر الأسباب الداعية في حقهم كالتقريع بالعجز والاستنزال عن الرياسات والتكليف بالانقياد فهذا الصرف خارق للعادة فيكون معجزاً. وقال المرتضى من الشيعة: بل صرفهم بأن سلبهم العلوم التي يحتاج إليها في المعارضة يعني أن المعارضة والإتيان بمثل القرآن يحتاج إلى علوم يقتدر بها عليها، وكانت تلك العلوم حاصلة لهم لكنه تعالى سلبها عنهم فلم يبق لهم قدرة عليها. قوله: (وقيل بالصرفة) رد عليه بأن الأنسب حينئذ ترك الاعتبار ببلاغته لأنه كلما كان أنزل في البلاغة كان عدم تيسر المعارضة أبلغ في الإعجاز.
First Observatory - Fourth Objective: On Establishing the Prophethood of Muhammad. Regarding the possible ranks of eloquence: (such) means that its being in the high, extraordinary degree is established, because whoever examines the Quran from among those knowledgeable in eloquence finds all its arts in it, from conveying many meanings with few words, and from types of emphasis, kinds of simile and representation (i.e., striking a likeness), and categories of metaphor, good openings and closures of speech, good rhymes (fasila), precedence and postponement, conjunction and disjunction appropriate to the context, and its being free from poor (i.e., weak) wording, anomalous (outside analogy), and rare (deviant from usage) — to other types of eloquence. Such that, i.e., he finds it containing the arts of eloquence such that the examiner of it (i.e., of the Quran and its constructions), the distinguisher between the arts of eloquence, does not see a single type of them (i.e., of those arts) except he finds it in the Quran in the best possible way. So the Quran includes all of them, not leaving any of them. And no one of the eloquent speakers who have reached the pinnacle of eloquence among the pure Arabs, even if he exhausts his capacity and ability in embellishing his speech, can (achieve) more than one or two types of it (i.e., of the mentioned arts of eloquence). And perhaps if he sought another (i.e., another type), it would not assist him (i.e., would not cooperate with him or be feasible for him). Al-Amidi said: The most eloquent of the Arabs and the most skilled in literature from the masters of poetry, prose, and sermons—his utmost is to exclusively possess one type of eloquence, such that if he sought another in his speech it would not assist him and he would be deficient therein. But the Quran contains them all. And whoever is more knowledgeable in Arabic (i.e., the language of the Arabs and its arts of eloquence) is more knowledgeable in the miraculous nature (i'jaz) of the Quran, which branches from its eloquence. Al-Qadi al-Baqillani said: It (i.e., the aspect of its i'jaz) is the combination of two matters: the extraordinary style (al-nazm al-gharib) and its being at the highest degree of eloquence. And it was said: It is its informing of the unseen, like (And they will after their defeat overcome in a few years) (al-Rum 3-4); He informed of the Romans' victory over the Persians within three to nine years, and it occurred as He informed. And that is much, known by examining the Quran and its reports about future matters that occurred accordingly. And it was said: The aspect of its i'jaz is its lack of discrepancy and contradiction despite its length and extension; they held to that by His saying: (Had it been from other than Allah, they would have found therein much contradiction) (al-Nisa 82). And it was said: Its i'jaz is by al-sarfa (divine prevention), meaning that the Arabs were capable of speech like the Quran before the mission, but Allah prevented them from opposing it. They differed on the modality of al-sarfa. The master Abu Ishaq from us and al-Nazzam from the Mu'tazila said: Allah prevented them from it despite their ability, by turning their motivations away from it, despite their innate disposition toward it, especially when motives for opposition were present, such as rebuke for inability, removal from leadership, and obligation of submission. So this prevention is miraculous (khariq al-'ada) and thus a miracle. And al-Murtada from the Shi'a said: Rather, He prevented them by stripping them of the sciences needed for opposition, meaning that opposing and producing something like the Quran requires sciences by which one can accomplish it, and those sciences had been available to them, but Allah removed them, so they no longer had ability. The saying: (And it was said by al-sarfa) is refuted by the fact that then it would be more appropriate to disregard its eloquence, because the lower its eloquence, the greater the prevention would be a miracle.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage enumerates the various scholarly opinions on the precise aspect (wajh) of the Quran's miraculous inimitability (i'jaz). It begins by describing the Quran's eloquence in detail: it contains all arts of eloquence—concision, emphasis, simile, metaphor, good structure, rhymes, word order, and freedom from weak or anomalous expressions. The author explains that no human orator can achieve more than one or two of these arts, quoting al-Amidi to that effect. Then he presents five views on wajh al-i'jaz: (1) Al-Qadi al-Baqillani combines the extraordinary style and superlative eloquence. (2) Some say it is the report of the unseen, citing the Romans' victory. (3) Others say it is the lack of contradiction (quoting Quran 4:82). (4) The theory of al-sarfa (divine prevention): God prevented the Arabs from opposing the Quran despite their ability. Two sub-views are given: Abu Ishaq and al-Nazzam say God turned their motivations away, while al-Murtada (Shi'i) says God removed their knowledge. (5) A marginal note refutes al-sarfa: if God simply prevented them, then low eloquence would make the prevention more miraculous, which is inappropriate for the Quran's high eloquence.

Key Terms
إعجاز
غيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What example is given for the Quran's reports of the unseen?
A) The victory of the Persians over the Romans
B) The victory of the Romans over the Persians
C) The splitting of the moon
D) The conquest of Mecca

Key Takeaway
The Quran's accurate predictions of future events, as exemplified by the Roman victory, are presented as a proof of its miraculous nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Quran predicted a specific event and it happened exactly. Do you truly believe that every word in the Quran is from Allah? If yes, why do you still hesitate to fully submit to its commands in your daily life? What area of your life are you holding back, knowing that the Quran's truthfulness is confirmed?
?
You have just learned that the Quran's reports of the unseen are a miracle. How does this affect the way you approach the Quran's descriptions of the afterlife, paradise, and hell? Are you living as if these are as certain as the Roman victory? If not, what needs to change?
?
The Quran's prophecies are evidence for everyone. But have you personally experienced the truth of the Quran in your own life? Reflect on a time when a Quranic verse or teaching proved true for you. If you cannot think of one, what is stopping you from testing it with sincerity?

What Is Kalam?
beginner
3 min
Lack of Contradiction as Miracle Proof
Presents the opinion that the Quran's lack of contradiction is a miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the Quran's internal consistency and lack of contradiction, despite its length, is a sign of its divine origin and a facet of its inimitability.

Core Idea
The Quran's consistency without contradiction proves it is from God.

This lesson presents the view that the Quran's miracle is its freedom from contradiction, as stated in the Quran itself (4:82). Proponents argue that if the Quran were from other than Allah, it would contain many discrepancies, especially given its length and diversity of topics. This is another proposed aspect of i'jaz.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on lack of contradiction
Read the Arabic and translation stating that the aspect of i'jaz is the absence of discrepancy and contradiction.
The verse (4:82) is cited as evidence.
2
Understand the argument from length
Note that the Quran is extensive and covers many subjects, yet remains consistent.
Human writings of similar length typically contain contradictions.
3
Connect to divine origin
Recognize that this consistency points to a single divine source.
This argument is also used elsewhere in Islamic theology.

?
